What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Refining the Definitions of Motion and Rest
Commentator refines motion/rest definitions to avoid first-kawn problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refines the definitions of motion and rest to avoid the problem of the first kawn being miscategorized.

Core Idea
Motion is a kawn not preceded in the same location but preceded in another.

The commentator begins by addressing a problem: if motion is simply a kawn not preceded in the same location, then the very first kawn of a substance (which has no prior kawn anywhere) would be motion, which is denied. He therefore refines the definition by adding the condition that motion must be preceded by a kawn in a different location.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with the initial definition
Read the passage and notice the problem: if motion is just a kawn not preceded in the same location, then the first kawn of a new substance would be motion, which is not accepted.
The commentator calls this the 'first-instant' problem.
2
Understand the refined definition
The commentator states that motion is a kawn that is not preceded by a kawn in the same location but is preceded by a kawn in another location. This avoids the first-instant problem.
3
See how rest is defined
Rest is a kawn preceded by another kawn in the same location. This aligns with common sense: rest is remaining in place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن تكون مسبوقة بالحصول في ذلك الحيز، إلا أن تكون مسبوقة بالحصول في حيز آخر) كما مر إذ على هذا لا يكون الكون الثاني حركة لأنه مسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز، ويكون الكون الأول حركة عن المكان السابق مع كونه سكوناً في هذا المكان والحاصل أنا لا نعتبر في السكون المسبوقية بكون آخر حتى يكون الكون في أول زمان الحدوث سكوناً، ونلتزم حينئذ أن تكون الحركة مركبة من السكنات، لكنّا نعتبر في الحركة عدم المسبوقية بالكون في ذلك الحيز، ولا نكتفي بما مر من كونها مسبوقة بالكون في حيز آخر حتى لا يلزمنا أن يكون الكون الثاني حركة، وإنما حملنا عبارة الكتاب على اعتبار الأمرين معاً في الحركة، إذ لو حملت على اعتبار الأول فقط كما هو ظاهرها لزم أن يكون الكون في أول زمان الحدوث حركة ولا قائل به، ثم أورد على جوابه اشكالاً بقوله : ( وحينئذ ) أي حين اعتبر في الحركة ما ذكر اندفع ذلك الإشكال لكن ( لا) تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات ) لأن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءاً للحركة وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون أن جميع أجزاء الحركة سكنات لأن كل سكون حركة، فحركة، وإلا فسكون فلكون الأول في المكان الثاني من حيث إيجابه لاختصاصه بالمكان الثاني مماثل للكون الثاني فيه فيكون سكونا، وليس مماثلاً من حيث الخروج من الحيز الأول فلا يكون الكون الثاني حركة فاندفع الإشكال الثاني، وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار الشارح بقوله : ويكون الكون الأول المكان السابق إلخ، وبما حررنا ظهر أن ما قيل أن الكلام إلزامي لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين، وبأن الكون الثاني سكون كما يستلزم كون الأول سكونا كذلك كون الأول حركة، فلا يتجه الجواب المذكور ليس بشيء، كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( حتى يكون الكون إلخ ) غاية للنفي لا للمنفي . عن قوله : ( ولا نكتفي إلخ نعتبر في الحركة مجموع الأمرين ونقول : الحركة الكون الأول في المكان الثاني والسكون الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني والكون الأول في المكان الأول . قوله : ( حتى لا يلزمنا إلخ غاية لقوله : نعتبر في الحركة لا لقوله : ولا نكتفي كما توهم ) فإنه فائدة ما ذكره بقوله : إذ لو حملت على اعتبار الأول فقط . قوله: (وهو مردود إلخ قيل الظاهر أن معنى قوله : وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة سكنات لزوم كون الحركة مجموع سكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنياً على قولهم بتماثل الحصولين، مجموع الكلام إلزامي لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول كذلك فلا يتجه حينئذ الجواب المذكور . قوله: (ولا) نكتفي بما مر من كونها مسبوقة إلخ) فيه بحث إذ قد سبق أن مرادهم بالسبق السبق بالاتصال فيكتفي بما مر فيعدم لزوم كون الكون الثاني حركة . قوله: (وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون إلخ) فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد لو كان وجه قول يجب أن يكون جزءاً للحركة، وهو ظاهر فإن الكون في أول الحدوث سكون عندهم وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) والنزاع) في أن الكون في أول زمان الحدوث سكون أو ليس بسكون (لفظي ) فإنه إن فسر السكون بالحصول في المكان مطلقاً كان ذلك الكون سكوناً، ولزم تركب الحركة من السكنات لأنها مركبة من الأكوان الأول في الأحياز كما عرفت، وإن فسر بالكون المسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز لم يكن ذلك الكون سكوناً ولا حركة، بل واسطة بينهما ولم يلزم أيضاً تركب الحركة من السكنات، فإن الكون الأول في المكان الثاني أعني الدخول فيه هو عين الخروج من المكان الأول وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول ، فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن يعتبروا فيه عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول الثاني هو حركة بالاتفاق فقد اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك المعنى، إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله من الآمدي، أو لعدم وجود الاشتراك في المتماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم، وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب الثاني لا يتأتى من جانبهم لمنافاته لقولهم: بتركب الحركة من السكنات وهذا كلام حق لايرد عليه قوله : وهو مردود انتهى. ولا يخفى اندفاعه بما حررناه سابقاً من أن تماثل الكون الأول بالثاني من حيث إيجابه الاختصاص بالحيز الثاني لا يستلزم تماثله من حيث إيجابه الخروج من الحيز الأول حتى يلزم أن لا يكون الحركة مركبة من السكنات . قوله : ( وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) أي إذا بقي ذلك الجوهر في ذلك المكان أو العدم في الآن الثاني بخلاف ما إذا انتقل منه الآن إلى جزء آخر فإن مجموع الكونين حركة عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من السكنات . المصنف، وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات أن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءاً للحركة كما زعمه والظاهر أن معنى قوله وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات، أن لزوم كون : الحركة مجموع السكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنياً على لزوم ذلك لقولهم بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول كذلك على ما تحققت فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن اعتبروا فيها عدم المسبوقية فيه بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول وهو حركة بالاتفاق اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله مع الآمدي أو بعدم وجوب اشتراك التماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع السكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب المذكور لاينافي من جانبهم وهذا كلام عليه قوله : وهو مردود إلخ غاية ما في الباب أن يكون في العبارة أدنى مسامحة فتدبر. قوله: (وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) الظاهر أنّ هذا إنما هو إذا انعدم الكائن في الآن الثاني، وإلا فإذا حدث جوهر في حيز ثم انتقل منه إلى حيز يليه وانعدم فيه، فالحركة مجموع الحصولين عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من الأكوان فافهم.
...to be preceded by existence in that location, unless it is preceded by existence in another location) as has passed, for on this basis the second kawn would not be motion because it is preceded by another kawn in that location, and the first kawn would be motion from the previous place while being rest in this place. The conclusion is that we do not consider in rest the being preceded by another kawn so that the kawn at the first time of occurrence would be rest, and we then commit that motion is composed of rests, but we consider in motion the non-being preceded by a kawn in that location, and we are not satisfied with what has passed of its being preceded by a kawn in another location so that the second kawn would not be motion, and we interpreted the wording of the book as considering both matters together in motion, for if it were interpreted as considering only the first, as its apparent meaning, it would entail that the kawn at the first time of occurrence would be motion, and no one says that. Then he raised an objection to his answer by saying: (And then) i.e., when motion is considered as mentioned, that objection is repelled, but (no) would motion be the sum of rests, because the second kawn is rest and not a part of motion, and it is rebutted by their claim that all parts of motion are rests because every rest is motion, so motion, otherwise rest. For the first kawn in the second place, in terms of its requiring specification to the second place, is similar to the second kawn therein, so it would be rest, and it is not similar in terms of departure from the first place, so the second kawn is not motion, so the second objection is repelled. And what we mentioned is indicated by the commentator's saying: and the first kawn [is] the previous place, etc. And from what we have refined, it appears that what was said that the speech is argumentative for those who say the similarity of the two existences, and that the second kawn being rest entails the first being rest, likewise the first being motion, so the mentioned answer is not valid, is nothing, as is not hidden. His saying: (so that the kawn etc.) is a limit for the negation, not for the negated. Regarding his saying: (and we are not satisfied etc.) we consider in motion the combination of the two matters and say: motion is the first kawn in the second place, and rest is the second kawn in the second place, and the first kawn is in the first place. His saying: (so that it does not entail us etc.) is a limit for his saying: we consider in motion, not for his saying: and we are not satisfied, as was imagined, for it is the benefit of what he mentioned by his saying: for if it were interpreted as considering only the first. His saying: (and it is rebutted etc.) It is said that the apparent meaning of his saying: And then motion would not be rests is that the entailment of motion being the sum of rests and their commitment to it was based on their saying about the similarity of the two existences, the sum of the speech is argumentative for those who say the similarity of the two existences and that the second being rest entails the first likewise, so then the mentioned answer is not valid. His saying: (and we are not satisfied with what passed of its being preceded etc.) There is discussion in it, for it has preceded that their meaning by precedence is precedence by connection, so what passed is sufficient, so the entailment of the second kawn being motion is invalid. His saying: (and it is rebutted by their claim etc.) There is discussion in it, because this only follows if the reason for the saying must be that it is a part of motion, and it is apparent, for the kawn at the first occurrence is rest according to them and not a part of motion at all) and the dispute) is whether the kawn at the first time of occurrence is rest or not rest (verbal), for if rest is interpreted as existence in a place absolutely, then that kawn would be rest, and the composition of motion from rests would be entailed because it is composed of the first kawans in the places as you know; and if it is interpreted as a kawn preceded by another kawn in that location, then that kawn would be neither rest nor motion, but an intermediary between them, and the composition of motion from rests would also not be entailed, for the first kawn in the second place, i.e., entering into it, is exactly the departure from the first place. And because the second being rest entails the first, so when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the non-being preceded by existence in that location while it is like the first kawn and the second is motion by agreement, they have admitted the invalidity of that meaning, either by the invalidity of similarity as transmitted from al-Amidi, or because of the lack of sharing in the two similar things, so this does not entail for them that motion is the sum of rests while they admit this entailment. In summary, the gist of the objection he raised is that the second answer does not come from their side because it contradicts their saying about the composition of motion from rests, and this is a correct statement that is not refuted by his saying: 'and it is rebutted.' End. And it is not hidden that it is repelled by what we refined earlier: that the similarity of the first kawn to the second in terms of its requiring specification to the second place does not entail its similarity in terms of its requiring departure from the first place so that motion would not be composed of rests. His saying: (and it is not a part of motion at all) i.e., if that substance remains in that place or ceases in the second instant, unlike if it moves from it in the instant to another part, for then the sum of the two kawans is motion according to those who say motion is composed of rests. The author, and then motion would not be the sum of rests that the second kawn is rest and not a part of motion as he claimed. And the apparent meaning of his saying 'and then motion would not be the sum of rests' is that the entailment of motion being the sum of rests and their commitment to it was based on the entailment of that due to their saying about the similarity of the two existences and that the second being rest entails the first likewise, as you have realized. So when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the non-being preceded by existence in that location while it is like the first kawn and it is motion by agreement, they admitted the invalidity of that either by the invalidity of similarity as transmitted with al-Amidi or by the lack of necessary sharing of the two similar things, so this does not entail for them that motion is the sum of rests while they admit this entailment. In summary, the gist of the objection he raised is that the mentioned answer is not incompatible from their side, and this is a correct statement that is not [refuted]. His saying: 'and it is rebutted' etc. The most that can be said is that there is a slight tolerance in the expression, so contemplate. His saying: (and it is not a part of motion at all) the apparent is that this is only if the being ceases in the second instant, otherwise if a substance occurs in a place then moves from it to an adjacent place and ceases therein, then motion is the sum of the two existences according to those who say motion is composed of kawans, so understand.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a dense commentary on the definitions of motion (حركة) and rest (سكون) within the framework of al-kawn (spatial accident). It begins by refining a definition: motion is a kawn not preceded by another kawn in the same location, but it may be preceded by a kawn in a different location. Rest is a kawn preceded by another in the same location. The commentator argues that this definition avoids the problem that the very first kawn of a recently created substance would otherwise be motion, which is not accepted. However, this creates a new issue: if motion is defined only by non-precedence in the same location, then a substance's first appearance in a place (which has no prior kawn in that place) would be motion, but theologians deny that. So the definition must incorporate both conditions: motion is a kawn that is not preceded in the same location but is preceded in a different location. The passage then addresses an objection: if we accept that the first kawn in a location is rest (because it is not motion), then motion would be composed of successive rests (sakanat), because motion involves multiple kawans each of which is itself a rest in its location. The commentator rebuts this by noting that the first kawn in the second location (i.e., the beginning of motion) is similar to the second kawn (the subsequent rest) in requiring specification to the location, but it differs in involving departure from the previous location, so it is not identical to a rest. The argument delves into whether motion is composite or simple, citing different views. Finally, the commentator states that the dispute is verbal: if 'rest' is defined as any kawn in a place, then the first kawn is rest and motion is composed of rests; if 'rest' is defined as a kawn preceded by another in the same place, then the first kawn is neither rest nor motion but an intermediate state, and motion is not composed of rests. The text concludes by defending the coherence of the answer against further objections, emphasizing that similarity in one aspect does not entail similarity in all aspects.

Key Terms
كون
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is simply any kawn not preceded in the same location.
Correction: The commentator shows that motion also requires being preceded in another location; otherwise, the first kawn would be motion, which is denied.

Checkpoint
According to the refined definition, what additional condition distinguishes motion from rest?
A) Motion must be preceded by a kawn in the same location.
B) Motion must be preceded by a kawn in a different location.
C) Motion must not be preceded by any kawn.
D) Rest must be preceded by a kawn in a different location.

Key Takeaway
Motion is defined by two conditions: it is not preceded by a kawn in the same location, but it is preceded by a kawn in another location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator refined definitions to avoid a logical absurdity. Are there areas of your own faith—such as your understanding of divine attributes or qadr—where you have accepted oversimplified definitions that lead to inner contradictions? What specific step will you take to seek clarity?
?
If every first moment of existence is a state of rest, what does that teach you about the beginning of your spiritual journey? How can you cultivate patience and stillness before you move in acts of worship, trusting that Allah has placed you first in a state of rest?

What Is Kalam?

Motion as Sum of Rests: Objection and Rebuttal
Commentator rebuts claim that motion is composed of rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that motion would be composed of rests under the refined definition and how the commentator rebuts it by distinguishing between the aspects of specification and departure.

Core Idea
The first kawn in a new location is not a rest because it involves departure from the previous location.

An objection arises: if motion involves a first kawn that is not preceded in that location, and that first kawn is considered rest (by some), then motion would be a sum of rests. The commentator rebuts by noting that the first kawn has an aspect of departure that a pure rest lacks.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage to see the objection: if the first kawn in a new location is rest (because it is not motion), then motion, which involves many such kawans, would be composed of rests.
This objection assumes that every kawn in a location that is not preceded in that location is rest.
2
Find the rebuttal
The commentator argues that the first kawn is not exactly like a rest because it also involves departure from the previous location. This aspect makes it different.
The similarity in specification does not entail similarity in departure.
3
See the resolution
The commentator concludes that motion is not composed of rests because the first kawn is a unique intermediate state.

كذلك فلا يتجه حينئذ الجواب المذكور . قوله: (ولا) نكتفي بما مر من كونها مسبوقة إلخ) فيه بحث إذ قد سبق أن مرادهم بالسبق السبق بالاتصال فيكتفي بما مر فيعدم لزوم كون الكون الثاني حركة . قوله: (وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون إلخ) فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد لو كان وجه قول يجب أن يكون جزءاً للحركة، وهو ظاهر فإن الكون في أول الحدوث سكون عندهم وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) والنزاع) في أن الكون في أول زمان الحدوث سكون أو ليس بسكون (لفظي ) فإنه إن فسر السكون بالحصول في المكان مطلقاً كان ذلك الكون سكوناً، ولزم تركب الحركة من السكنات لأنها مركبة من الأكوان الأول في الأحياز كما عرفت، وإن فسر بالكون المسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز لم يكن ذلك الكون سكوناً ولا حركة، بل واسطة بينهما ولم يلزم أيضاً تركب الحركة من السكنات، فإن الكون الأول في المكان الثاني أعني الدخول فيه هو عين الخروج من المكان الأول وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول ، فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن يعتبروا فيه عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول الثاني هو حركة بالاتفاق فقد اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك المعنى، إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله من الآمدي، أو لعدم وجود الاشتراك في المتماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم، وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب الثاني لا يتأتى من جانبهم لمنافاته لقولهم: بتركب الحركة من السكنات وهذا كلام حق لايرد عليه قوله : وهو مردود انتهى. ولا يخفى اندفاعه بما حررناه سابقاً من أن تماثل الكون الأول بالثاني من حيث إيجابه الاختصاص بالحيز الثاني لا يستلزم تماثله من حيث إيجابه الخروج من الحيز الأول حتى يلزم أن لا يكون الحركة مركبة من السكنات . قوله : ( وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) أي إذا بقي ذلك الجوهر في ذلك المكان أو العدم في الآن الثاني بخلاف ما إذا انتقل منه الآن إلى جزء آخر فإن مجموع الكونين حركة عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من السكنات . المصنف، وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات أن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءاً للحركة كما زعمه والظاهر أن معنى قوله وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات، أن لزوم كون : الحركة مجموع السكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنياً على لزوم ذلك لقولهم بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول كذلك على ما تحققت فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن اعتبروا فيها عدم المسبوقية فيه بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول وهو حركة بالاتفاق اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله مع الآمدي أو بعدم وجوب اشتراك التماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع السكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب المذكور لاينافي من جانبهم وهذا كلام عليه قوله : وهو مردود إلخ غاية ما في الباب أن يكون في العبارة أدنى مسامحة فتدبر. قوله: (وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) الظاهر أنّ هذا إنما هو إذا انعدم الكائن في الآن الثاني، وإلا فإذا حدث جوهر في حيز ثم انتقل منه إلى حيز يليه وانعدم فيه، فالحركة مجموع الحصولين عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من الأكوان فافهم.
...to be preceded by existence in that location, unless it is preceded by existence in another location) as has passed, for on this basis the second kawn would not be motion because it is preceded by another kawn in that location, and the first kawn would be motion from the previous place while being rest in this place. The conclusion is that we do not consider in rest the being preceded by another kawn so that the kawn at the first time of occurrence would be rest, and we then commit that motion is composed of rests, but we consider in motion the non-being preceded by a kawn in that location, and we are not satisfied with what has passed of its being preceded by a kawn in another location so that the second kawn would not be motion, and we interpreted the wording of the book as considering both matters together in motion, for if it were interpreted as considering only the first, as its apparent meaning, it would entail that the kawn at the first time of occurrence would be motion, and no one says that. Then he raised an objection to his answer by saying: (And then) i.e., when motion is considered as mentioned, that objection is repelled, but (no) would motion be the sum of rests, because the second kawn is rest and not a part of motion, and it is rebutted by their claim that all parts of motion are rests because every rest is motion, so motion, otherwise rest. For the first kawn in the second place, in terms of its requiring specification to the second place, is similar to the second kawn therein, so it would be rest, and it is not similar in terms of departure from the first place, so the second kawn is not motion, so the second objection is repelled. And what we mentioned is indicated by the commentator's saying: and the first kawn [is] the previous place, etc. And from what we have refined, it appears that what was said that the speech is argumentative for those who say the similarity of the two existences, and that the second kawn being rest entails the first being rest, likewise the first being motion, so the mentioned answer is not valid, is nothing, as is not hidden. His saying: (so that the kawn etc.) is a limit for the negation, not for the negated. Regarding his saying: (and we are not satisfied etc.) we consider in motion the combination of the two matters and say: motion is the first kawn in the second place, and rest is the second kawn in the second place, and the first kawn is in the first place. His saying: (so that it does not entail us etc.) is a limit for his saying: we consider in motion, not for his saying: and we are not satisfied, as was imagined, for it is the benefit of what he mentioned by his saying: for if it were interpreted as considering only the first. His saying: (and it is rebutted etc.) It is said that the apparent meaning of his saying: And then motion would not be rests is that the entailment of motion being the sum of rests and their commitment to it was based on their saying about the similarity of the two existences, the sum of the speech is argumentative for those who say the similarity of the two existences and that the second being rest entails the first likewise, so then the mentioned answer is not valid. His saying: (and we are not satisfied with what passed of its being preceded etc.) There is discussion in it, for it has preceded that their meaning by precedence is precedence by connection, so what passed is sufficient, so the entailment of the second kawn being motion is invalid. His saying: (and it is rebutted by their claim etc.) There is discussion in it, because this only follows if the reason for the saying must be that it is a part of motion, and it is apparent, for the kawn at the first occurrence is rest according to them and not a part of motion at all) and the dispute) is whether the kawn at the first time of occurrence is rest or not rest (verbal), for if rest is interpreted as existence in a place absolutely, then that kawn would be rest, and the composition of motion from rests would be entailed because it is composed of the first kawans in the places as you know; and if it is interpreted as a kawn preceded by another kawn in that location, then that kawn would be neither rest nor motion, but an intermediary between them, and the composition of motion from rests would also not be entailed, for the first kawn in the second place, i.e., entering into it, is exactly the departure from the first place. And because the second being rest entails the first, so when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the non-being preceded by existence in that location while it is like the first kawn and the second is motion by agreement, they have admitted the invalidity of that meaning, either by the invalidity of similarity as transmitted from al-Amidi, or because of the lack of sharing in the two similar things, so this does not entail for them that motion is the sum of rests while they admit this entailment. In summary, the gist of the objection he raised is that the second answer does not come from their side because it contradicts their saying about the composition of motion from rests, and this is a correct statement that is not refuted by his saying: 'and it is rebutted.' End. And it is not hidden that it is repelled by what we refined earlier: that the similarity of the first kawn to the second in terms of its requiring specification to the second place does not entail its similarity in terms of its requiring departure from the first place so that motion would not be composed of rests. His saying: (and it is not a part of motion at all) i.e., if that substance remains in that place or ceases in the second instant, unlike if it moves from it in the instant to another part, for then the sum of the two kawans is motion according to those who say motion is composed of rests. The author, and then motion would not be the sum of rests that the second kawn is rest and not a part of motion as he claimed. And the apparent meaning of his saying 'and then motion would not be the sum of rests' is that the entailment of motion being the sum of rests and their commitment to it was based on the entailment of that due to their saying about the similarity of the two existences and that the second being rest entails the first likewise, as you have realized. So when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the non-being preceded by existence in that location while it is like the first kawn and it is motion by agreement, they admitted the invalidity of that either by the invalidity of similarity as transmitted with al-Amidi or by the lack of necessary sharing of the two similar things, so this does not entail for them that motion is the sum of rests while they admit this entailment. In summary, the gist of the objection he raised is that the mentioned answer is not incompatible from their side, and this is a correct statement that is not [refuted]. His saying: 'and it is rebutted' etc. The most that can be said is that there is a slight tolerance in the expression, so contemplate. His saying: (and it is not a part of motion at all) the apparent is that this is only if the being ceases in the second instant, otherwise if a substance occurs in a place then moves from it to an adjacent place and ceases therein, then motion is the sum of the two existences according to those who say motion is composed of kawans, so understand.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a dense commentary on the definitions of motion (حركة) and rest (سكون) within the framework of al-kawn (spatial accident). It begins by refining a definition: motion is a kawn not preceded by another kawn in the same location, but it may be preceded by a kawn in a different location. Rest is a kawn preceded by another in the same location. The commentator argues that this definition avoids the problem that the very first kawn of a recently created substance would otherwise be motion, which is not accepted. However, this creates a new issue: if motion is defined only by non-precedence in the same location, then a substance's first appearance in a place (which has no prior kawn in that place) would be motion, but theologians deny that. So the definition must incorporate both conditions: motion is a kawn that is not preceded in the same location but is preceded in a different location. The passage then addresses an objection: if we accept that the first kawn in a location is rest (because it is not motion), then motion would be composed of successive rests (sakanat), because motion involves multiple kawans each of which is itself a rest in its location. The commentator rebuts this by noting that the first kawn in the second location (i.e., the beginning of motion) is similar to the second kawn (the subsequent rest) in requiring specification to the location, but it differs in involving departure from the previous location, so it is not identical to a rest. The argument delves into whether motion is composite or simple, citing different views. Finally, the commentator states that the dispute is verbal: if 'rest' is defined as any kawn in a place, then the first kawn is rest and motion is composed of rests; if 'rest' is defined as a kawn preceded by another in the same place, then the first kawn is neither rest nor motion but an intermediate state, and motion is not composed of rests. The text concludes by defending the coherence of the answer against further objections, emphasizing that similarity in one aspect does not entail similarity in all aspects.

Key Terms
كون
حركة
سكون
تماثل الحصولين
مجموع السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is simply a sum of rests.
Correction: The commentator shows that the first kawn in motion is not a rest because it has an aspect of departure, so motion is not composed of rests.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the first kawn in a new location is not a rest?
A) It is not preceded by any kawn.
B) It involves departure from the previous location.
C) It is similar to the second kawn.
D) It is not motion.

Key Takeaway
The first kawn in motion is not a rest because it involves departure from the previous location, so motion is not composed of rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you have been 'at rest' spiritually or morally—stagnant in your growth. What specific 'departure' from that location are you avoiding? Will you take that step today?
?
The objector reduced motion to a collection of rests. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes reduce worship to a series of actions (prayers, fasting) without the dynamic movement of the heart? How can you ensure that each act includes a real departure from negligence?
?
If the first kawn in a new location is not rest because it involves departure, consider your next good deed. Is it just another instance of the same, or does it involve a genuine departure from your previous state? What needs to change for it to be true motion?

Definition of Conjunction (Ijtima') and Separation (Iftiraq)
Al-Jurjani defines ijtima' and iftiraq based on interposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of conjunction (ijtima') and separation (iftiraq) in kalam physics and why they are based on the possibility of interposition, including the allowance of void space.

Core Idea
Two substances are in conjunction if no third substance can possibly interpose between them.

Al-Jurjani begins by clarifying two fundamental spatial relations between substances: conjunction and separation. These terms are not about mere distance but about the possibility of a third substance intervening. Understanding this subtle distinction requires considering the theologians’ allowance of empty space (void). This passage lays out the precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if it is such that...'
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Al-Jurjani defines iftiraq and ijtima'. Note that the criterion is whether a third substance can interpose.
The key phrase is 'يمكن أن يتخلل' (can interpose).
3
Understand 'possibility' vs 'actuality'
The author specifies 'possibility of interposition' rather than actual interposition. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
Consider the role of void (khala') in this distinction.
4
Consider the concept of void
Learn that theologians allow empty space (khala'). How does the possibility of void affect the definitions? If void exists, interposition is possible, so it would be separation.
Void is not a substance; it is an empty place.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to solidify your grasp of the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأول وهو حصول الجوهر في الجوهر في الحيز بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر فإن كان بحيث يمكن أن يتخلل بينه وبين ذلك الآخر جوهر ثالث فهو الافتراق وإلا فهو الاجتماع وإنما قلنا إمكان التخلل دون وقوع التخلل لجواز أن يكون بينهما خلاء أي مكان خال عن المتحيز عند المتكلمين فإنهم يجوزونه
As for the first [meaning of conjunction], it is the occurrence of a substance in a substance in space relative to another substance. If it is such that a third substance can interpose between it and that other, then it is separation (iftiraq); otherwise it is conjunction (ijtima'). We said 'possibility of interposition' rather than 'actual interposition' because it is possible that there is between them a void (khala'), i.e., an empty place devoid of spatial occupation, according to the theologians (al-mutakallimun), for they allow it.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in kalam physics: conjunction (ijtima') and separation (iftiraq). The definition is given in terms of two substances in relation to each other within a spatial framework (al-hayyiz). The criterion for distinguishing between them is whether a third substance can be interposed between the two. If interposition is possible, it is iftiraq; if not, it is ijtima'. The author notes that we consider possibility rather than actuality, because according to the theologians, empty space (khala') may exist between the two substances; if we required actual interposition, empty space would make conjunction impossible even when no substance is actually present. The commentary in the margins clarifies that the conditional clause 'if it is such that' refers to the state of being in a particular spatial relation relative to another atom.

Key Terms
اجتماع (ijtima')
افتراق (iftiraq)
خلاء (khala')
جوهر (jawhar)
حيز (hayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think ijtima' requires actual contact without any void between the two substances.
Correction: Actually, ijtima' is defined by the impossibility of a third substance interposing, regardless of whether void exists. If void is present, interposition is possible, so it would be separation, not conjunction.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what is the criterion for conjunction (ijtima') between two substances?
A) Actual interposition of a third substance is impossible
B) No void exists between them
C) A third substance cannot possibly interpose
D) The two substances are in the same location

Key Takeaway
Conjunction and separation are defined by whether a third substance can possibly interpose, not by actual interposition or the absence of void.

Kawn and Its Four Types: One Reality, Conceptual Distinctions
Al-Jurjani defines kawn and explains its four types as one reality with conceptual distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the four types of kawn (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) are not distinct essences but one reality differentiated only by conceptual attributes.

Core Idea
The four types of 'being-in-space' are one reality distinguished by conceptual features only.

This passage from al-Jurjani’s Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a foundational point in atomistic physics: the nature of spatial occurrence (kawn). He argues that what we call motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are all just one type of being-in-space, with the differences between them being purely mental (i'tibariyya) rather than real. This insight prevents us from multiplying entities unnecessarily and clarifies the theologian's view of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice how al-Jurjani begins by stating that kawn’s existence is self-evident through sense testimony.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify the statement that ‘the distinguishing features … are conceptual matters, not real differentiae’. This is the central point that the four types are really one.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: motion, rest (two views), conjunction, and separation. Understand how each is a case of kawn with a particular conceptual relation (precedence, interposition).
These examples make the abstract point concrete.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if the distinctions are merely conceptual, then the four types are not independent accidents but variations of a single reality. This aligns with the mutakallimun’s parsimony in positing accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكون أي الحصول في الحيز وجوده ضروري بشهادة الحس وكذا أنواعه الأربعة على رأي المتكلمين موجودة إذ حاصلها كما علمت من التقسيم عائد إلى الكون الذي هو نوع واحد في الحقيقة والمميزات التي بها تميزت تلك الأنواع بعضها عن بعض أمور اعتبارية لا فصول حقيقية منوعة نحو كونه مسبوقاً بكون آخر إما في مكان آخر كما في الحركة أو في ذلك المكان كما في السكون على رأي أو غير مسبوق به أي بكون آخر على معنى لا يعتبر كونه مسبوقاً بكون آخر كما في السكون على رأي آخر ونحو إمكان تخلل ثالث بينهما وعدمه في الافتراق والاجتماع ولا شبهة في أنّ هذه الأمور الاعتبارية لا وجود لها في الخارج
Al-kawn, i.e., occurring in space. Its existence is necessary by the testimony of sense (shahadat al-hiss), and likewise its four types, according to the theologians, exist. For their result, as you know from the division, reduces to al-kawn, which is in reality one type. The distinguishing features (mumayyizat) by which those types are differentiated from each other are conceptual (i'tibariyya) matters, not real differentiae. For example: its being preceded by another kawn, either in another place as in motion, or in that same place as in rest according to one view; or not being preceded by it, i.e., by another kawn, meaning that its being preceded by another kawn is not considered, as in rest according to another view; and likewise the possibility of a third interposing between them or not, in separation and conjunction. And there is no doubt that these conceptual matters have no existence in the external world.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of al-kawn ('being-in-space') and its four types. First, the existence of kawn is self-evident through sense perception (al-hiss). According to the mutakallimun, there are four types of kawn: motion, rest, conjunction, and separation. However, upon analysis, all four are reducible to a single reality—kawn—because the distinctions among them are conceptual (i'tibariyya) rather than real differentiae (fusul haqiqiyya). The author gives examples: motion is a kawn preceded by another kawn in a different place; rest is a kawn preceded by another kawn in the same place (according to one view) or not preceded at all (according to another); conjunction and separation are distinguished by whether a third atom can interpose. Because these distinguishing features are merely conceptual, they have no external existence. The commentary clarifies that 'by testimony of sense' means the intellect judges existence based on sense, whether the kawn itself is directly sensible or not.

Key Terms
كون (kawn)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)
أمور اعتبارية (umur i'tibariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are four fundamentally different types of accidents with no common essence.
Correction: The passage explains that they are all the same reality (kawn) and only differ by conceptual features like precedence or interposition, not by real differentiae.

Checkpoint
Why are the four types of kawn considered really one type?
A) Because they all exist in space
B) Because their distinguishing features are conceptual, not real differentiae
C) Because sense testimony confirms one existence
D) Because motion and rest are opposites

Key Takeaway
The four types of kawn (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) are not distinct realities but one reality of being-in-space, differentiated only by conceptual attributes that have no external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that conceptual labels like 'motion' and 'rest' have no external reality—they are mental distinctions. Where in your own life do you mistake human labels and categories for ultimate truths? How can you train your heart to see the single reality beneath the diversity of your experiences, especially when dealing with changes in your spiritual state?
?
If 'kawn' is one reality, what does that imply about the unity underlying Allah's creation? Are you content to observe only the surface differences between things, or do you seek the deeper oneness? What one step can you take today to look beyond conceptual divisions and recognize the hand of the One Creator in all situations?
?
Al-Jurjani relies on 'testimony of sense' to assert the existence of kawn. How trustworthy are your senses in matters of faith? Have you ever trusted a sensory impression (e.g., feeling distant from Allah) more than the intellectual truth that Allah is always near? What would it mean for you to submit your sensory perceptions to the guidance of sound intellect and revelation?

The Verbal Dispute About Motion in a Moving Ship
Analyzes whether an object stationary in a moving ship is itself moving, and resolves it as a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a theological-philosophical dispute about motion can be resolved as a verbal dispute when the definition of a key term (space) is clarified.

Core Idea
What seems like a real dispute about motion is actually a disagreement about the meaning of 'space.'

This lesson examines a classic example used in kalam to show how many disagreements are merely verbal. By analyzing the definitions of the term 'space' (al-ḥayyiz), we see that the dispute about whether an object stationary in a moving ship is itself moving collapses into a clarification of terms. The passage demonstrates the importance of precise definitions in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses technical terms like 'intermediate substance' and 'space'.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage presents two opposing views: one says the stationary object is not moving, the other says it is moving. Find where each opinion is stated.
The second opinion includes an argument about separating from surrounding air.
3
Understand the resolution
The author declares the dispute to be verbal (nizaʿ lafẓī). Ask yourself: why does the definition of space (ḥayyiz) determine the answer?
This is the key insight of the lesson.
4
Examine the three definitions of space
The passage lists three interpretations of space: hypothetical dimension, surrounding substances, and supporting base. For each, see how the judgment about motion changes.
This step may require re-reading the commentary.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this matters for kalam methodology. How does clarifying definitions resolve disputes?
Think about other theological disagreements that might be verbal.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع: الاختلاف في حركة الكون
سيصرح به ( وكذلك اختلف في المستقر في السفينة المتحركة ) فقيل : ليس بمتحرك كالجوهر المتوسط، فقيل : متحرك وكيف لا ) وأنه أولى بالحركة) من الجوهر المتوسط إذ هو يفارق بعض السطح المحيط به أعني الجواهر الهوائية التي أحاطت به من فوقه بخلاف المتوسط فإنه لا يفارق شيئا من السطح المحيط به ( والحق أنه نزاع لفظي يعود إلى تفسير الحيز كما نبهتك عليه ) آنفاً فإن فسر بالبعد المفروض كان المستقر في السفينة المتحركة كالجوهر المتوسط لخروج كل منهما حينئذ من حيز إلى حيز آخر وإن فسر بالجواهر المحيطة الوسطاني مفارقاً لحيزه أصلاً وأما المستقر المذكور فإنه يفارق بعضاً من الجواهر المحيطة به دون بعض وإن فسر الحيز بما اعتمد عليه ثقل الجوهر كما هو المتعارف عند الجمهور لم يكن المستقر مفارقاً لمكانه أصلاً .
The Fourth Observatory – The Fourth Objective: The Disagreement Concerning the Motion of the Universe. He will explicitly state: (And likewise they disagreed about the stationary [object] in a moving ship). So it was said: It is not moving, like the intermediate substance. And it was said: It is moving, and how not? And it is more deserving of motion than the intermediate substance, because it separates from some of the surrounding surface, namely the aerial substances that surround it from above, unlike the intermediate which does not separate from any of the surrounding surface. (And the truth is that it is a verbal dispute that goes back to the interpretation of space (al-ḥayyiz), as I reminded you) earlier. For if it is interpreted as the hypothetical dimension (al-buʿd al-mafrūḍ), then the stationary [object] in the moving ship is like the intermediate substance, because each of them then exits from one space to another space. And if it is interpreted as the surrounding substances, the intermediate [substance] separates from its space entirely, while the mentioned stationary [object] separates from some of the surrounding substances but not from others. And if space is interpreted as that which supports the weight of the substance, as is customary among the majority, then the stationary [object] does not separate from its place at all.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage discusses the dispute over whether an object that remains stationary in a moving ship is itself in motion. Two opinions are given: first, it is not moving, like a substance in the middle of the ship that is surrounded on all sides; second, it is moving, because it shifts relative to the surrounding air. The passage then declares this to be a verbal dispute (nizāʿ lafẓī) that depends on the definition of 'space' (ḥayyiz). The author then outlines three definitions: (1) space as a hypothetical dimension (al-buʿd al-mafrūḍ); in this case the stationary object in the ship does change its space because it moves relative to the dimension, just as the intermediate substance does. (2) space as the surrounding substances; here the intermediate substance entirely leaves its surrounding substances, while the stationary object only leaves some of them, so opinions differ. (3) space as the base supporting the weight of the substance (the common understanding); in this case the stationary object does not leave its place at all because its base remains the same. Thus the dispute reduces to which definition of space one adopts.

السفينة المتحركة
الجوهر المتوسط
الحيز
البعد المفروض
الجواهر المحيطة
نزاع لفظي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this is a genuine physical disagreement about whether the object is truly moving.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is merely a verbal dispute that disappears once the definition of 'space' is settled.

Checkpoint

The dispute about motion in a moving ship is resolved by clarifying the definition of 'space,' illustrating that many disagreements in kalam are verbal rather than substantive.

Clarification: The Dispute is About Real Meaning, Not Convention
Explains that the dispute over motion is not mere terminology but about the true meaning of motion across languages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disagreement over motion in kalam is not a mere terminological dispute but a genuine investigation into the essence of motion.

Core Idea
The dispute over motion is about the real essence, not arbitrary labels.

The commentator argues that the dispute is not simply about what we choose to call 'motion'. If it were merely conventional, then the entire theological reasoning about motion would lose its force. This passage clarifies that the scholars are actually seeking the true essence (māhiyya) for which the word 'motion' was originally coined across all languages.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: إطلاق, وضع, اصطلاح, الماهية.
The passage references Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid and al-Mawāqif.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the view that the dispute is merely terminological (from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid) and the commentator's refutation that it is about real essence.
Look for the phrase 'ليس نزاعاً راجعاً إلى الوضع والاصطلاح'.
3
Understand the refutation
Understand why the commentator insists it is not conventional: rational proofs would be meaningless if definitions were arbitrary. The goal is to discover the true essence of motion.
The commentator emphasizes that the dispute is about the correct ascription (إطلاق) of the term to the real essence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركة بإزائها هي لفظ من قوله : (اطلق اسم الحركة إلخ لا أنه اصطلح على ذلك بل لأن الماهية التي وضع تبدل المحاذاة سواء كان مبدأ المتبدل فيه أو في غيره فلا يرد ما في شرح المقاصد من أن كونه نزاعاً في التسمية، ليس على ما بنبغي لأن ماذكره الأستاذ في بيان الحيز أو الحركة أنه هذا أو ذاك ليس اصطلاحاً منهم على أن نجعله اسماً لذلك، وإلا لما كان لجعله المسائل الكلامية والاستدلال عليه بالدلالة العقلية معنى تحقيقاً للماهية التي وضع لفظ الحيز والحركة وما يرادفه من جميع اللغات بإزائها وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار الشارح بقوله : أطلق أي نسبة التسمية في المتن بمعنى الإطلاق لا بمعنى الوضع فهذا نزاع في إطلاق اللفظ، وأن المعنى الذي يطلق لفظ الحركة في جميع اللغات ما هو وليس نزاعاً راجعاً إلى الوضع والاصطلاح.

قوله : ( لأنه نزاع في التسمية قال في شرح المقاصد : وما ذكره في المواقف من أن هذا نزاع في التسمية ليس على ما ينبغي، لأن ما ذكره الأستاذ وغيره في بيان الحيز والحركة أنه هذا أو ذاك ليس اصطلاحاً ،منهم على أنا نجعله اسماً لذلك الحيز وإلا لما كان لجعله من المسائل العلمية أو الاستدلال عليه بالأدلة العقلية معنى بل تحقيق للماهية التي وضع لفظ الحيز والحركة أو ما يرادفه من جميع اللغات بإزائهم وإثبات ذاتياتها بعد تصورها بالحقيقة حين الحكم بأن هذا في حيز وذلك في آخر وأن هذا متحرك وذاك ساكن.
The word 'motion' is [used] with respect to it from his statement: (He applied the name 'motion' etc.) — not that he stipulated it by convention, but because the essence for which the word was coined is the change of alignment, whether the origin of the change is in the moving object or in something else. So what is said in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid — that it is a dispute about terminology — is not as it should be, because what al-Ustadh and others mentioned in explaining space or motion — that it is this or that — is not a convention from them that we make it a name for that. Otherwise, making it one of the theological questions and reasoning upon it with rational proofs would have no meaning. Rather, it is an investigation into the essence for which the word 'space' and 'motion' and their equivalents in all languages were coined, and an affirmation of their essential properties after conceptualizing them truly when judging that this is in a space and that is in another, and that this is moving and that is at rest.

His statement: (Because it is a dispute about terminology) — he said in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: And what was mentioned in al-Mawāqif — that this is a dispute about terminology — is not as it should be, because what al-Ustadh and others mentioned in explaining space or motion — that it is this or that — is not a convention from them that we make it a name for that space. Otherwise, making it one of the scientific questions or reasoning upon it with rational proofs would have no meaning. Rather, it is an investigation into the essence for which the word 'space' or 'motion' or their equivalents in all languages were coined, and the affirmation of their essential properties after conceptualizing them truly when judging that this is in a space and that is in another, and that this is moving and that is at rest.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the notion that the dispute is merely terminological. It explains that al-Ustadh's claim is not an arbitrary convention (iṣṭilāḥ) but a true definition: motion is fundamentally a change of alignment (tabaddul al-muḥādāh), regardless of whether the object itself moves or its surroundings move. The passage refutes the idea that it is purely a verbal dispute, as suggested in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, by arguing that if it were just convention, then theological arguments about motion would be meaningless. Instead, the scholars are aiming to discover the real essence (māhiyya) for which the word 'motion' was originally coined in all languages. Thus the disagreement is actually about the true meaning of motion, not about arbitrary labels. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the dispute is about the correct ascription (iṭlāq) of the term to the real essence, not about stipulative definition (waḍʿ).

Agreement on the Possibility of an Indivisible Atom
States the agreement among those who affirm atoms on the possibility of a single atom surrounded by six atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among proponents of atomism in kalām regarding the possibility of an indivisible atom being surrounded by six atoms from all directions.

Core Idea
The indivisible atom is not isolated; it can be fully surrounded by six others.

In this lesson, we examine a key point of agreement among early kalām theologians who accepted atomism. They concurred that a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard) can exist with six atoms in contact, one on each side. This principle set the foundation for their discussions about the structure of matter. The passage also notes a dissenting report, showing that even within the atomic framework, there were variant views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms and the structure of the agreement and exception.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إلا ما نقل عن بعض المتكلمين'.
2
Identify the key components
Break down the passage into: the subject (those who affirm atoms), the claim (agreement on possibility), the condition (surrounded by six), and the exception.
3
Understand the significance of the six directions
The passage explicitly lists the six directions: right, left, front, back, above, below. This shows the atom is fully encompassed.
4
Reflect on the exception
Consider why some theologians might have denied this possibility. What implications does it have for their overall atomistic theory?
The exception is noted but not explained, leaving room for further study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : اتفاق القائلين على وجود جوهر فرد ] اتفق القائلون بالأكوان على أنه ( يجوز وجود جوهر فرد محفوف بستة جواهر ملاقية له ( من جهاته الست إلا ما نقل عن بعض المتكلمين) من ( أنه منع ذلك ) قوله : ( من جهاته الست ) أي الأيمن والأيسر والأمام والخلف والفوق والتحت.
The Fifth Objective: Agreement of the Proponents on the Existence of an Indivisible Atom. Those who affirm the accidents (al-akwān) agreed that (it is permissible for an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) to exist, surrounded by six atoms in contact with it (from its six directions), except for what is reported from some theologians) that (he forbade that). His statement: (from its six directions) meaning the right, left, front, back, above, and below.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a point of agreement among the majority of kalām scholars who affirm atoms (jawāhir) and accidents (akwān): they hold that it is possible (jāʾiz) for a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard) to be completely surrounded by six other atoms, one on each of its six sides. This is the default position, except for a report that some theologians denied this possibility. The commentary clarifies that 'from its six directions' means the six sides: right, left, front, back, above, and below. The passage thus establishes a consensus with a known exception.

The Atom's 'Separation' When Touching: A Verbal Dispute
Al-Jurjānī resolves a dispute about atom separation as verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjānī considers the disagreement about whether an atom is 'separate' from another atom when touching from one side to be a verbal dispute, and how the definition of mubāyana determines the correct view.

Core Idea
Definitions, not reality, drive this dispute.

Al-Jurjānī presents a subtle classification within the properties of the indivisible atom. Theologians disagreed about whether an atom touching another from one side can be called 'separate' from the other side. Al-Jurjānī shows that the disagreement hinges entirely on what we mean by 'separation'—making it a verbal dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two positions. Notice the structure: the question, two views, and the resolution.
Pay attention to the technical terms: mubāyana, mumāssa, mujāwara.
2
Identify the two views and their basis
The first view holds that the atom is separate from the other side because there is no touch there. The second view (al-Ustādh) holds that it is not separate because touch from that side is impossible at that moment.
Al-Ustādh is likely Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī.
3
Understand the resolution
Al-Jurjānī classifies this as a verbal dispute. The truth depends on whether we define mubāyana as requiring the possibility of touching. If yes, the second view is correct; if not, the first view is correct.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثالث : الجوهر الفرد ( إذا ماس ) جوهراً آخر ( من جهة، فهل يقال : إنه مباين) لذلك الجوهر الآخر ) من الجهة الأخرى ) كما ذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين (لعدم) حصول المماسة في تلك الجهة الأخرى ( أم لا يقال ذلك كما ذهب إليه الأستاذ لأنه لا يمكن المجاورة ) والمماسة ( من تلك الجهة الأخرى ( حينئذ ) أي حين هو مماس له من الجهة الأولى ( وهذا نزاع لفظي ( لأنه إن اعتبر في المباينة إمكان المماسات في تلك الحالة، فالحق هو تلك الحالة، فالحق هو الثاني وإن لم يعتبر فالحق هو الأول.
The third branch: the indivisible atom, when it touches another atom from one side, is it said that it is separate (mubāyin) from that other atom from the other side – as some theologians held, due to the absence of touching on that other side – or is it not said that, as the master (al-Ustādh) held, because contiguity (mujāwara) and touch (mumāssa) from that other side are impossible at that time, i.e., when it is touching it from the first side? And this is a verbal dispute (nizāʿ lafẓī), because if in separation (mubāyana) one considers the possibility of touching in that state, the truth is the second view; and if one does not consider it, the truth is the first view.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a classification: a dispute concerning the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). When one atom touches another from one side, theologians disagreed about whether it is called 'separate' (mubāyin) from the other side. Some said yes, because there is no touch on that other side. Al-Ustādh (likely Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī) said no, because at that moment, contiguity and touch from the other side are impossible. The author then resolves the dispute as a verbal dispute (nizāʿ lafẓī): it depends on whether the definition of 'mubāyana' requires the possibility of touching. If yes, then the second view (al-Ustādh's) is correct; if not, the first view is correct. The author does not side definitively but clarifies the logical structure.

جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)
مماسة (mumāssa)
مباينة (mubāyana)
مجاورة (mujāwara)
نزاع لفظي (verbal dispute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this dispute is about a real metaphysical property of atoms.
Correction: Al-Jurjānī clarifies it is merely a verbal dispute (nizāʿ lafẓī) hinging on the definition of mubāyana.

D) The second view is always correct.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjānī resolves the dispute over the atom's separation upon touching as a verbal dispute, showing that theological disagreements often hinge on definitions.

The Fourth Branch: Permissibility of Separation and Disjunction
Al-Jurjānī defends the permissibility of separation by clarifying the condition of contiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjānī defends the permissibility of separation among atoms by distinguishing between alternating and simultaneous contiguity.

Core Idea
Separation requires only the possibility of alternating contiguity, not simultaneous union.

In the fourth branch, the discussion turns to whether atoms can be separated and disjoined from each other entirely. Some theologians argue that separation is impossible because it would require the possibility of contiguity between wholes, which they deem impossible. Al-Jurjānī clarifies the condition, showing that only alternating contiguity is needed. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument without over-analyzing.
The passage is short but dense—notice the structure: statement, objection, response.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some theologians say separation is impossible. Why? Because they think contiguity between wholes is impossible, and separation requires such possibility.
This is the 'قيل' (it was said) part.
3
Understand the author's response
Al-Jurjānī says 'it suffices' that the possibility of contiguity exists alternately (badalan). This means the atoms can be contiguous at different times, not simultaneously.
The key distinction is between alternating (بدلاً) and simultaneous (على الاجتماع).
4
Reflect on the resolution
The objection is based on misinterpretation: they assumed contiguity must be simultaneous. The author shows that alternating contiguity is sufficient for the concept of separation.
This resolves the dispute without denying the impossibility of simultaneous contiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الرابع : يجوز المباينة والافتراق في جملة جواهر (العالم بحيث لا يتصف شيء منها بالاجتماع مع غيره كما إذا تبدلت وزال تركيبها بالكلية ( وقيل: لا ) يجوز (إذ لا تجوز المجاورة بين الكل ولا بد في المباينة من إمكان المجاورة قال المصنف : ( ويكفي) يعني في الوصف بالمباينة (جوازها) أي جواز المجاورة بين الكل (بدلاً ) ولا شبهة في هذا الجواز، وإنما الممتنع هو المجاورة بين الكل على الاجتماع
The fourth branch: separation (mubāyana) and disjunction (iftirāq) are permissible in the set of atoms of the world in such a way that none of them is characterized by composition (ijtimāʿ) with another, as if they are displaced and their composition is completely removed. And it was said: it is not permissible, because contiguity (mujāwara) between wholes is not permissible, and in separation (mubāyana) there must be possibility of contiguity. The author said: 'It suffices' – meaning in the description of separation – 'its possibility', i.e., the possibility of contiguity between wholes alternately (badalan). And there is no doubt about this possibility; the impossible is only contiguity between wholes simultaneously (ʿalā al-ijtimāʿ).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth branch: the question of whether separation and disjunction among a collection of atoms are possible. Some theologians hold that it is not possible because separation (mubāyana) requires the possibility of contiguity (mujāwara) between the two wholes, and contiguity between two wholes is impossible. The author (al-Jurjānī) and the author of the matn (al-Ījī) argue that for separation, it suffices that contiguity is possible alternately (badalan) – that the parts can be contiguous at different times. The impossibility is only of simultaneous contiguity. The passage clarifies that the condition is the possibility of alternation, not simultaneity.

Key Terms
مباينة
افتراق
مجاورة
اجتماع
بدلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think separation requires the possibility of immediate union, i.e., simultaneous contiguity.
Correction: The passage shows that only alternating contiguity is required; simultaneous contiguity is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the author say suffices for describing two atoms as 'separate' (mubāyana)?
A) Their simultaneous contiguity.
B) The impossibility of any contiguity.
C) The possibility of contiguity between them alternately.
D) Their actual disjunction in space.

Key Takeaway
Separation among atoms is permissible because it only requires the possibility of alternating contiguity, not simultaneous union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel distant from Allah or from a loved one, do you assume that distance is permanent or that closeness is impossible? This lesson shows that separation does not preclude the possibility of alternating closeness. How can this truth reshape your hope and actions in strained relationships today?
?
The opponent made a logical error: they assumed that because simultaneous contiguity is impossible, separation is impossible. In your own life, have you ever concluded that because an ideal scenario is unattainable, any lesser scenario is also invalid? Will you now repent of that perfectionism and accept that Allah's allowance of alternation is sufficient?
?
This lesson carefully distinguishes between two types of contiguity. In your spiritual walk, do you conflate 'being with Allah all at once' (simultaneous, which is impossible in this life) with 'being with Him at different times' (alternating, which is possible through prayer and remembrance)? Commit today to value the alternating closeness that Allah grants you.

The Sixth Purpose: Are All Akwān Contradictory? (Part 1)
al-Jurjānī presents the first view that any two akwān are contradictory with its reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first position that any two akwān are contradictory and the reasoning based on specification to one or two locations.

Core Idea
Two akwān cannot coexist because they would force the atom into impossible states.

In this passage, al-Jurjānī presents the first of two views on whether any two akwān are always contradictory. The first group, led by al-Qāḍī, does not consider touch (mumāssa) as a distinct type of kawn. They argue that any two akwān are opposites and can only exist in succession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the first position
Find where al-Jurjānī describes those who do not make touch a distinct state, and see what they claim about akwān.
2
Follow the reasoning
The reasoning is based on two hypothetical possibilities: two akwān either specify the atom to one location or two. Each leads to impossibility.
3
Understand the conclusion
Because both possibilities are impossible, the akwān cannot exist together; they are contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : كل كونين متضادان ] يتصور ( من لم يجعل المماسة (كوناً قائماً بالجوهر كالقاضي وأتباعه (أطلق القول بتضاد الأكوان ) على معنى أن كل كونين فهما متضادان ) لأن الكونين المجتمعين فرضاً ( إما أن يوجبا تخصيص الجوهر بحيز واحد أو بحيزين والأول اجتماع المثلين) لأن كل واحد من الكونين مثل للآخر، والمثلان ضدان لا يجتمعان بل لا وجودهما في الجوهر إلا على سبيل التعاقب، كما إذا كان مستقراً في حيز واحد أكثر من زمان، فإن الكون المتجدد في الزمن الثاني مماثل للكون الموجود في الزمن الأول لقيام كل واحد منهما مقام في الآخر تخصيص الجوهر بذلك الحيز ( والثاني يوجب حصول الجوهر في آن واحد في حيزين فامتنع اجتماع الكونين مطلقاً فهما متضادان ( ومن (جعلها أي المماسة (كونا) مخصوصاً قائماً بالجوهر، وجوز قيام المماسات المتعددة بالجوهر الواحد ( كالشيخ والأستاذ فلم يجعلها ) أي الأكوان (أضداداً ولا مماثلة بل (مختلفة لجواز اجتماعها في جوهر واحد
The sixth purpose: every two akwān are contradictory. It is considered: Whoever does not make touch (mumāssa) a state (kawn) subsisting in the atom – like al-Qāḍī and his followers – he declares absolutely the contradiction of the akwān, meaning that every two akwān are contradictory. Because two akwān, hypothetically combined, either necessitate the specification of the atom to one location (ḥayyiz) or to two locations. The first is the union of two likes (mithlayn), because each of the two akwān is a likeness of the other, and two likes are opposites (ḍiddān) that do not combine; rather, they only exist in the atom in succession, as when it is settled in one location for more than one moment, for the kawn renewed in the second moment is similar to the kawn existing in the first moment because each takes the place of the other in specifying the atom to that location. The second necessitates the existence of the atom in one instant in two locations, so the combination of the two akwān is absolutely impossible; thus they are contradictory. And whoever makes touch (mumāssa) a specific state (kawn makhṣūṣ) subsisting in the atom, and allows the subsistence of multiple touches in one atom – like al-Shaykh and al-Ustādh – he does not make the akwān opposites nor likes, but rather different (mukhtalifa) because their combination in one atom is possible.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a classification: the sixth purpose (maqṣad) regarding the alleged contradiction of akwān. There are two main positions. The first group (al-Qāḍī and followers) does not consider touch (mumāssa) as a distinct 'state' (kawn); they maintain that any two akwān are contradictory (muḍāddān). The reasoning: two akwān imagined together must either specify the atom to one location (which would be the union of two likes, and likes are opposites that cannot coexist, only succeed each other) or specify it to two locations (impossible, as one thing cannot be in two places at once). Therefore, they are contradictory. The second group (al-Shaykh and al-Ustādh) treats touch as a specific type of kawn and allows multiple touches in one atom; for them, akwān are not opposites or likes but merely different (mukhtalifa), since they can coexist. The passage is thus a distinction based on how one defines the nature of touch.

Key Terms
أكوان (akwān)
مماسة (mumāssa)
كون (kawn)
تضاد (taḍād)
مثل (mithl)
تخصيص بالحيز (specification to a location)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to those who do not consider touch as a distinct state, why are two akwān contradictory?
A) Because they are always different types.
B) Because they would either require two likes in the same location or the atom in two locations.
C) Because touch cannot be a kawn.
D) Because they cannot exist in time.

Key Takeaway
The first position holds that any two akwān are contradictory because they cannot coexist without leading to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjānī argues that two likes cannot coexist because they are opposites. Reflect on your own life: what ‘likes’ (habits, beliefs, attachments) do you try to combine that cannot truly coexist? What contradiction are you living with that you need to confront?
?
The argument shows that if two akwān were combined, the atom would have to be in two places at once—an absurdity. In what area of your faith or practice are you attempting to hold two mutually exclusive positions? What would it cost you to let one go?
?
The passage teaches that states (akwān) only succeed one another, never coexist. Consider your spiritual states: do you expect to have both fear and hope simultaneously in perfect balance, or do you accept that they must alternate? How might this understanding change how you approach your worship today?

The Sixth Purpose: The Second View on Akwān (Part 2)
al-Jurjānī presents the second view that akwān are merely different.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view that akwān are merely different, not opposites, because touch is a distinct state that can coexist.

Core Idea
When touch is a distinct kawn, akwān are just different—not opposites.

In this passage, al-Jurjānī presents the second view, held by al-Shaykh and al-Ustādh. They treat touch (mumāssa) as a specific type of kawn and allow multiple touches in one atom. As a result, akwān are not opposites or likes; they are merely different, since they can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second group
Look for 'ومن جعلها أي المماسة كونا مخصوصاً' – those who make touch a special state.
2
Understand their position
They allow multiple touches to subsist in one atom, meaning akwān can coexist.
3
Contrast with the first view
For them, akwān are not opposites or likes, only different, because coexistence is possible.

Definition of Motion and the Potential/Actual Principle
Al-Iji defines motion and argues that nothing can be purely potential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of motion as the first perfection of what is potential, and why nothing can be purely potential.

Core Idea
Motion is the actualization of potential; pure potentiality is non-existence.

This passage introduces a foundational concept in Islamic philosophy: the definition of motion. Al-Iji begins by dividing body into moving and stationary, then presents Aristotle's definition of motion as the first perfection of what is potential. He then argues that nothing can be purely potential, otherwise it would be non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Notice the two-part structure: definition and proof.
2
Identify the two categories of body
The first sentence divides body into moving and stationary. Find how stillness is defined.
Stillness is the absence of motion in a thing capable of motion.
3
Understand Aristotle's definition
The second sentence gives the definition of motion. Identify the key terms: 'first perfection', 'what is potential', and note the qualification 'of a potential substrate in so far as it is potential'.
This definition emphasizes that motion is the actualization of a potential, not just any change.
4
Grasp the proof against pure potentiality
The third sentence argues that nothing can be purely potential in all respects. Why would that be pure non-existence?
If something were potential in its very existence, it would not actually exist.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this principle is important for later arguments about motion and change in the universe.
This principle grounds that every existent has actuality, preventing infinite regress of potentialities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحكماء الجسم إما أن يكون متحركاً، أو لا يكون، والثاني هو الساكن لأنَّ السكون عندهم كما مر، عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك. والحركة عرفها أرسطو ومن تابعه بأنها كمال أول لما بالقوة، أي لمحل يكون بالقوة من حيث هو بالقوة. وبيان ذلك أن كل ما هو بالقوة من الموجودات فإنه لا يكون بالقوة من كل وجه وإلا فعدم محض إذ يكون حينئذ بالقوة في كونه موجوداً فلا يكون موجوداً.
The philosophers said: The body is either moving or not; the second is stationary because stillness for them, as mentioned, is the absence of motion from that which is apt to move. Motion was defined by Aristotle and those who followed him as 'the first perfection of what is potential,' i.e., of a substrate that is potential in so far as it is potential. The explanation of this is that everything that is potential among existents is not potential in every respect; otherwise it would be pure non-existence, for then it would be potential in its existence and thus would not exist.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It first divides body into moving and stationary, defining stillness as the absence of motion in something capable of motion. Then it gives Aristotle's definition of motion as 'the first perfection of what is potential,' meaning the actualization of a potential substrate qua potential. The author then provides a proof: anything that is purely potential in all respects would be non-existent, because if something were potential in its very existence, it would not actually exist. Therefore, every existent must have some actuality; it cannot be purely potential.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
قوة
فعل
كمال أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think motion is a single type, but the definition distinguishes between potential and actual states.
Correction: Motion is the actualization of a potential; it is not a substance but a process of actualization.

Checkpoint

A) A substance that moves
B) The first perfection of what is potential
C) The absence of stillness
D) A category of quantity

Key Takeaway
Motion is the actualization of a potential substrate; nothing can be purely potential because that would be non-existence.
