Motion's Self-Evident Nature and Its Verbal Definitions
Al-Jurjani explains motion's self-evident nature and the insufficiency of its definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion cannot be given a real definition and why the definitions proposed are merely verbal.

Core Idea
Motion is known by direct awareness, not by definition.

In this passage, al-Jurjani explains that motion is a self-evident concept that cannot be truly defined. He shows that all attempts to define it are merely verbal descriptions based on concomitants. This discussion sets the stage for the subsequent disagreement about which category motion belongs to.

Guided Path
1
Understand why motion is self-evident
Read how al-Jurjani describes motion as known by overall essential knowledge, removing particularities.
This is a key epistemological point in kalam.
2
Distinguish real vs. verbal definitions
Note his claim that no real definition is possible; only verbal definitions exist.
Real definition captures essence; verbal definition gives concomitants.
3
Identify the listed definitions
Find the six definitions: emergence from potentiality to actuality, first perfection, intermediateness, cutting distance, orientation, performance.
4
Recognize the warning
Understand that using these definitions to argue for a category is based on a false assumption that they are real definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في مباحث الأين إلخ) أي المباحث التي لها نوع تعلق بالأين لأنها مباحث الحركة التي تقع فيه وتخصيص التعلق بالأين مع أنَّ لها تعلقاً بالمقولات الأخر أيضاً لكون وقوعها فيه بديهياً متفقاً عليه، ثم اعلم أنَّ الحركة معلومة بالكنه الإجمالي بديهية بحذف المشخصات من جزئياتها المعلومة بإعانة النفس، فلا يمكن تعريفها الحقيقي فالتعريفات التي ذكروها من الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل تدريجاً وكمال أول لا بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة والتوسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى وقطع المسافة والتوجه والتأدي كلها تعريفات لفظية باللوازم ولا يفيد تصور حقيقتها فلذلك بعد الاتفاق على صحة تلك التعريفات اختلفوا في أنها مقولة برأسها أو داخلة في واحد من المقولات وأنها من مقولة الانفعال أو الإضافة أو الكيف أو أنها مقولة وقعت فيه فالاستدلال بشيء من تلك التعريفات على دخولها في مقولة توهم ناشئ من توهم كون ذلك التعريف حداً . قوله : ( في مباحث الأين على رأي الحكماء) ظاهر كلامه يشعر بأنَّ الحركة من مقولة الأين على رأي الحكماء، وليس بمتعين نعم هي عند المتكلمين القائلين بأنها السكونان في آنين في مكانين أو الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني من مقولة الأين، وأما عند الحكماء قيل : هي مع قطع النظر عما يقع فيه إن فسرت بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج، فمن مقولة الانفعال وإن فسرت بالتوسط فمن مقولة الإضافة وإن فسرت بقطع المسافة فمن مقولة الفعل، وأما تفسيرها بأنها كمال أول إلخ، فلا يظهر منها أنها من أي المقولات عند هذا المفسر، ويمكن أن يكون قوله على رأي الحكماء متعلقاً بالمباحث لا بالأين فالأينية حينئذ على رأينا والمباحث على رأي الفلاسفة، لكن لا يخلو عن بعد لأنَّ المباحث تعم الحركة في الكم والكيف، وليس شيء
His saying: 'concerning the investigations of where etc.' means the investigations that have a kind of relation to 'where' because they are investigations of motion that occurs in it. The specification of the relation to 'where' despite its relation also to other categories is because its occurrence in it is self-evident and agreed upon. Then know that motion is known by an overall essential knowledge, self-evident by removing the particularizing aspects from its known particulars with the help of the soul. Thus it cannot be given a real definition. The definitions they mentioned — emergence from potentiality to actuality gradually, first perfection not of the potential qua potential, the intermediate between beginning and end, cutting distance, orientation, and performance — are all verbal definitions through concomitants and do not give a conception of its reality. Therefore, after agreeing on the soundness of those definitions, they differed whether it is a category on its own or included in one of the categories, and whether it is from the category of passion, relation, quality, or a category in which it occurs. Arguing from any of these definitions for its inclusion in a category is a fancy arising from the fancy that that definition is a definition per genus et differentiam. His saying: 'in the investigations of where according to the philosophers' — his apparent speech suggests that motion is from the category of 'where' according to the philosophers, but it is not predetermined. Rather, according to the theologians who say that it is two stillnesses in two instants in two places, or the first being in the second place, it is from the category of 'where'. As for the philosophers, it is said: abstracting from what it occurs in, if it is interpreted as emergence from potentiality to actuality gradually, then it is from the category of passion; if interpreted as intermediateness, then from the category of relation; if interpreted as cutting distance, then from the category of action. As for its interpretation as first perfection etc., it is not apparent from it which category it belongs to according to this interpreter. It is possible that his saying 'according to the philosophers' is connected to 'investigations' not to 'where', in which case the 'where-ness' is according to us and the investigations are according to the philosophers, but this is not free from remoteness because the investigations include motion in quantity and quality, and there is nothing [to support it].
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'in the investigations of where.' It first explains that these investigations concern motion as it occurs in place. Despite motion relating to other categories, its relation to place is self-evident. The commentator then notes that motion cannot be truly defined; all proposed definitions are merely verbal. Consequently, scholars differ on whether motion is an independent category or belongs to another category like passion, relation, or quality. The second part of the passage addresses the phrase 'according to the philosophers' in the heading. It points out that the author's wording might suggest motion is from the category of 'where' for philosophers, but this is not definite. For theologians, motion (as two stillnesses) is indeed from 'where'. For philosophers, depending on the definition, motion can be passion, relation, or action. The commentator also suggests an alternative: the phrase 'according to the philosophers' might modify 'investigations' rather than 'where', meaning the investigations follow philosophical methods while the category 'where' remains according to Islamic theologians.

Key Terms
الأين
الحركة
المقولة
التعريف الحقيقي
التعريف اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Such definitions are only verbal descriptions of concomitants, not real definitions of the essence.

Motion is self-evident; any attempt to define it only describes its attributes, not its reality.

The Disagreement on Motion's Category
Al-Jurjani discusses the categorization of motion according to theologians and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians and philosophers differ on which category motion belongs to, depending on their interpretations.

Core Idea
Motion's category varies with definition and school.

Al-Jurjani now addresses the phrase 'according to the philosophers' from the heading and clarifies that the categorization of motion depends on one's definition and school. Theologians place it in the category of 'where' because they define motion as two stillnesses in two places, while philosophers assign it to different categories like passion, relation, or action depending on how they interpret motion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial ambiguity
Read how the author's phrasing suggests motion is from 'where' for philosophers, but that is not definite.
2
The theologians' view
Learn that for theologians, motion as two stillnesses is from the category of 'where'.
3
Philosophers' views based on definitions
See how motion is placed in passion, relation, or action depending on the chosen definition.
4
Consider the alternative parsing
Explore the possibility that 'according to the philosophers' modifies 'investigations' rather than 'where'.
Al-Jurjani notes this is somewhat forced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في مباحث الأين إلخ) أي المباحث التي لها نوع تعلق بالأين لأنها مباحث الحركة التي تقع فيه وتخصيص التعلق بالأين مع أنَّ لها تعلقاً بالمقولات الأخر أيضاً لكون وقوعها فيه بديهياً متفقاً عليه، ثم اعلم أنَّ الحركة معلومة بالكنه الإجمالي بديهية بحذف المشخصات من جزئياتها المعلومة بإعانة النفس، فلا يمكن تعريفها الحقيقي فالتعريفات التي ذكروها من الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل تدريجاً وكمال أول لا بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة والتوسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى وقطع المسافة والتوجه والتأدي كلها تعريفات لفظية باللوازم ولا يفيد تصور حقيقتها فلذلك بعد الاتفاق على صحة تلك التعريفات اختلفوا في أنها مقولة برأسها أو داخلة في واحد من المقولات وأنها من مقولة الانفعال أو الإضافة أو الكيف أو أنها مقولة وقعت فيه فالاستدلال بشيء من تلك التعريفات على دخولها في مقولة توهم ناشئ من توهم كون ذلك التعريف حداً . قوله : ( في مباحث الأين على رأي الحكماء) ظاهر كلامه يشعر بأنَّ الحركة من مقولة الأين على رأي الحكماء، وليس بمتعين نعم هي عند المتكلمين القائلين بأنها السكونان في آنين في مكانين أو الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني من مقولة الأين، وأما عند الحكماء قيل : هي مع قطع النظر عما يقع فيه إن فسرت بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج، فمن مقولة الانفعال وإن فسرت بالتوسط فمن مقولة الإضافة وإن فسرت بقطع المسافة فمن مقولة الفعل، وأما تفسيرها بأنها كمال أول إلخ، فلا يظهر منها أنها من أي المقولات عند هذا المفسر، ويمكن أن يكون قوله على رأي الحكماء متعلقاً بالمباحث لا بالأين فالأينية حينئذ على رأينا والمباحث على رأي الفلاسفة، لكن لا يخلو عن بعد لأنَّ المباحث تعم الحركة في الكم والكيف، وليس شيء
His saying: 'concerning the investigations of where etc.' means the investigations that have a kind of relation to 'where' because they are investigations of motion that occurs in it. The specification of the relation to 'where' despite its relation also to other categories is because its occurrence in it is self-evident and agreed upon. Then know that motion is known by an overall essential knowledge, self-evident by removing the particularizing aspects from its known particulars with the help of the soul. Thus it cannot be given a real definition. The definitions they mentioned — emergence from potentiality to actuality gradually, first perfection not of the potential qua potential, the intermediate between beginning and end, cutting distance, orientation, and performance — are all verbal definitions through concomitants and do not give a conception of its reality. Therefore, after agreeing on the soundness of those definitions, they differed whether it is a category on its own or included in one of the categories, and whether it is from the category of passion, relation, quality, or a category in which it occurs. Arguing from any of these definitions for its inclusion in a category is a fancy arising from the fancy that that definition is a definition per genus et differentiam. His saying: 'in the investigations of where according to the philosophers' — his apparent speech suggests that motion is from the category of 'where' according to the philosophers, but it is not predetermined. Rather, according to the theologians who say that it is two stillnesses in two instants in two places, or the first being in the second place, it is from the category of 'where'. As for the philosophers, it is said: abstracting from what it occurs in, if it is interpreted as emergence from potentiality to actuality gradually, then it is from the category of passion; if interpreted as intermediateness, then from the category of relation; if interpreted as cutting distance, then from the category of action. As for its interpretation as first perfection etc., it is not apparent from it which category it belongs to according to this interpreter. It is possible that his saying 'according to the philosophers' is connected to 'investigations' not to 'where', in which case the 'where-ness' is according to us and the investigations are according to the philosophers, but this is not free from remoteness because the investigations include motion in quantity and quality, and there is nothing [to support it].
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'in the investigations of where.' It first explains that these investigations concern motion as it occurs in place. Despite motion relating to other categories, its relation to place is self-evident. The commentator then notes that motion cannot be truly defined; all proposed definitions are merely verbal. Consequently, scholars differ on whether motion is an independent category or belongs to another category like passion, relation, or quality. The second part of the passage addresses the phrase 'according to the philosophers' in the heading. It points out that the author's wording might suggest motion is from the category of 'where' for philosophers, but this is not definite. For theologians, motion (as two stillnesses) is indeed from 'where'. For philosophers, depending on the definition, motion can be passion, relation, or action. The commentator also suggests an alternative: the phrase 'according to the philosophers' might modify 'investigations' rather than 'where', meaning the investigations follow philosophical methods while the category 'where' remains according to Islamic theologians.

Key Terms
الأين
الحركة
الحكماء
المتكلمين
الانفعال
الإضافة
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all philosophers agree motion belongs to one category, e.g., passion.
Correction: Philosophers assign different categories depending on how they define motion.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what category does motion belong to for philosophers if it is defined as 'cutting distance'?
A) Passion
B) Relation
C) Action
D) Where

Key Takeaway
The categorization of motion is not uniform; it depends on the definition and the school of thought.

Defining 'The Moving Thing' and Defending the Definition
Defines 'al-mutaharrik' as having actual motion and rebuts an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'al-mutaharrik' is defined in kalam and why the definition must involve actual motion despite potential objections.

Core Idea
To be described by motion, you must actually be moving.

In this passage, the author defines the term 'the moving thing' (al-mutaharrik) and immediately defends it against a logical objection. The objection concerns whether the definition is trivial or false, and the rebuttal clarifies the intended meaning. This lesson examines the definition, the objection, and the philosophical principles underlying the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the literal translation. Notice the objection and rebuttal structure.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the objection's two horns.
2
Identify the objection
Find the two problematic interpretations of 'moving' that are rejected. Understand why each leads to a problem: trivial if actual, false if potential.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The author clarifies that 'moving' simply means 'described by motion' without specifying potentiality or actuality, but the motion itself must be actual. Reflect on why this is necessary.
Note the distinction between definition (conceptual principles) and inquiry (affirming a predicate).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : ( المتحرك ) أي الموصوف بالحركة ) له حركة بالفعل) حال
قوله : (أي الموصوف إلخ) أشار به إلى دفع مناقشة وهو أنه إن أريد المتحرك بالفعل فالحكم لغو، وإن أريد بالقوة فالحكم غير صحيح، ووجه الدفع أنَّ المراد الموصوف بالحركة من غير ملاحظة بالقوة والفعل، ولا شك أنَّ الموصوف بالحركة لا بد أن تكون حركة بالفعل إذ لا يمكن الاتصاف بالحركة المعدومة منهما أيضاً على رأي المتكلمين بل هم لا يقولون بهما كما سيجيء هذا، واعلم أن البحث في الاصطلاح إثبات المحمول للموضوع، فالتعريف ليس منها بل هو من المبادي التصورية إلا أن يعتبر الحكم الضمني.
And when you know this, we say: The moving thing—meaning the thing described by motion—has actual motion (as a state). His statement: (meaning the described thing etc.) he indicated by it the rebuttal of a criticism, namely that if what is meant by 'the moving thing' is the actual mover, the ruling is superfluous; and if what is meant is the potential mover, the ruling is incorrect. The rebuttal is that what is meant is the thing described by motion without regard to potentiality or actuality. And there is no doubt that the thing described by motion must have actual motion, because it is impossible to be described by non-existent motion. This is also according to the theologians—rather they do not affirm both as will come. And know that inquiry (al-bahth) in terminology is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, so definition is not from that, but rather from the conceptual principles (al-mabadi al-tasawwuriyya), unless the implicit judgment is considered.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-mutaharrik' (the moving thing) and then defends the definition. The base text states: 'The moving thing, i.e., the thing described by motion, has actual motion.' The commentary explains that this phrasing rebuts a potential objection: if 'moving' meant 'actually moving,' the statement would be trivial; if it meant 'potentially moving,' it would be false. The intended meaning is simply 'the thing described by motion' without specifying potentiality or actuality—yet the motion itself must be actual because one cannot be described by a non-existent motion. The commentary also notes that definition (ta'rif) belongs to the conceptual principles (mabadi tasawwuriyya), not to the inquiry of affirming a predicate for a subject (which is tasdiq), unless an implicit judgment is involved.

Key Terms
المتحرك (al-mutaharrik)
بالقوة (bi al-quwwa)
بالفعل (bi al-fi'l)
تعريف (ta'rif)
مبادي تصورية (mabadi tasawwuriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'moving' can refer to potential motion, and that the definition is acceptable either way.
Correction: The passage insists that to be described by motion, the motion must be actual because description requires existence, and potential motion is non-existent.

Checkpoint
What objection does the definition of al-mutaharrik address?
A) That motion must be either potential or actual.
B) That the definition is circular.
C) That the moving thing must have actual motion.
D) That the definition is from the tasawwuri principles.

Key Takeaway
A thing is described as 'moving' only if it has actual motion, not potential motion, and this definition avoids both triviality and falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Motion as First and Second Perfection
Defines motion as a perfection and distinguishes first and second perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion is defined as a perfection and the distinction between first perfection (motion itself) and second perfection (the endpoint).

Core Idea
Motion is a means to a further perfection, not an end itself.

This lesson explores the concept of motion as a perfection in Islamic philosophy. The author explains that motion is an occurrence after non-existence, and such an occurrence is a perfection. He clarifies that perfection means actuality, whether or not preceded by potentiality. Then he distinguishes between the motion itself as a first perfection and the endpoint as a second perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage silently, then refer to the literal English translation. Notice the key terms: الحركة, الكمال, الكمال الأول, الكمال الثاني.
Pay attention to how the author defines perfection as actuality, not necessarily preceded by potentiality.
2
Understand the Definition of Perfection
The passage says perfection (الكمال) means that which is actually attained. It can be preceded by potentiality (as in animal motion) or not (as in eternal motions). The term 'perfection' is used because potentiality is deficiency and actuality is complete relative to it.
The author emphasizes that prior potentiality is not required; only its conceptualization suffices.
3
Distinguish First and Second Perfection
The motion itself is called the first perfection (الكمال الأول) because it leads to the attainment of the endpoint, which is the second perfection (الكمال الثاني). The first perfection is a means; the second perfection is the end.
This distinction clarifies that motion is not an end in itself but a transition to a further state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتصافه بها (وهو) أي الحركة ( أمر حصل له بعد أن لم يكن حاصلاً له عند استقراره في مكانه أو على حاله ) فهو ( أي ذلك الأمر الحاصل بعد ما لم يكن ( كمال له ) أي للمتحرك (إذ معنى الكمال ذلك ) هذا إشارة إلى المطلق المذكور في ضمن المقيد أي معنى الكمال هو الحاصل بالفعل سواء كان مسبوقاً بالقوة، كما في حركات الحيوانات أو غير مسبوق بها كما في الكمالات الدائمة الحصول، والحركات الأزلية على رأي الفلاسفة، وإنما سمي الحاصل بالفعل كمالاً ، لأنَّ في القوة نقصاناً والفعل تام بالقياس إليها، وهذه التسمية لا تقتضي سبق القوة بل يكفيها تصورها وفرضها وقد يعتبر في مفهوم الكمال كونه لائقاً بما حصل فيه، لكنه ليس بمعتبر هاهنا إذ لا أن تكون الحركة لائقة بصاحبها ( وأنه ( أي ذلك الأمر الذي هو الحركة ( يؤدي) المتحرك ) إلى حصول ممكن آخر له وهو الحصول في المنتهى) مثلاً (فهذا ) الممكن الآخر ( كمال ثان ( إذا حصل بالفعل ) وذلك ) الأمر المؤدي إليه وهو الحركة الحاصلة ) كمال أول ) بالقياس إلى ذلك الممكن الذي يترتب عليه .
Its being described by it (i.e., motion) is an affair that occurs for it after not having occurred for it, upon its settling in its place or its state. So it (i.e., that affair occurring after not having been) is a perfection (kamal) for it, i.e., for the moving thing, because the meaning of perfection is that. This is an indication of the absolute mentioned within the restricted, i.e., the meaning of perfection is that which is actually attained, whether preceded by potentiality, as in the motions of animals, or not preceded, as in perpetual perfections and the eternal motions according to the philosophers. It is called 'perfection' because potentiality is deficiency and actuality is perfect relative to it. This naming does not require the prior existence of potentiality; rather, its conceptualization or assumption suffices. Sometimes the concept of perfection considers suitability for the subject, but it is not considered here because motion need not be suitable for its possessor. And that it (i.e., that affair which is motion) leads the moving thing to the attainment of another possible [state] for it, namely the attainment at the endpoint. So this other possible is a second perfection (kamal thani) when actually attained, and that which leads to it—i.e., the motion that occurs—is a first perfection (kamal awwal) relative to that possible which depends on it.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage defines motion as a perfection. It explains that motion is an occurrence after non-existence, and that this occurrence is a perfection for the moving thing. The meaning of 'perfection' (kamal) is simply that which is actually attained, regardless of whether it was preceded by potentiality (as in animal motions) or not (as in eternal perfections). The term 'perfection' is used because potentiality is deficiency, and actuality is complete relative to it. The passage further distinguishes between two perfections: the motion itself is the first perfection (kamal awwal), which leads to a second perfection (kamal thani)—the attainment of the endpoint. This distinction clarifies that motion is not an end in itself but a means to another possible state.

الكمال (al-kamal)
الكمال الأول (al-kamal al-awwal)
الكمال الثاني (al-kamal al-thani)
بالقوة (bi al-quwwa)
بالفعل (bi al-fi'l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perfection always requires prior potentiality.
Correction: The passage states that perfection (actual attainment) can be without prior potentiality, as in eternal motions. Only conceptualization of potentiality suffices.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first perfection (الكمال الأول)?
A) The motion itself.
B) The endpoint of motion.
C) The potentiality in the moving thing.
D) The actuality of the moving thing.

Key Takeaway
Motion is a first perfection that leads to a second perfection, namely the attainment of the endpoint.

Potentiality During Motion
Explains the necessity of potentiality during motion and discusses the definition's implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that during motion, part of the first perfection (motion) remains in potentiality, and that this potentiality arises from two aspects: the anticipated second perfection and the first perfection itself.

Core Idea
Motion requires potentiality: part of motion is always potential during change.

This passage from the commentary on the definition of motion explains a crucial condition: as long as something is moving, part of its motion remains in a state of potentiality. The author identifies two aspects from which this potentiality arises, setting the stage for understanding motion as a process involving both actuality and potentiality. The subsequent clarifications address the application of the definition and potential pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and frequent references to earlier statements.
2
Identify the main condition
Locate the sentence that states the necessity of potentiality during motion. What does the author say about the first perfection (motion) as long as the moving thing is moving?
3
Find the two aspects
The text says 'from two aspects' (من وجهين). Identify what these two aspects are: the anticipated second perfection and the first perfection itself.
The second perfection is the endpoint or goal of motion; the first perfection is the motion itself.
4
Examine the commentary on definition
Read the later part where the commentator discusses the definition of motion, the example of pleasure, and the issue of hylomorphic matter. Reflect on why these clarifications matter for the definition.
This part addresses potential objections and refines the understanding of perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه ( أي المتحرك ويجب ) مادام متحركاً) بالفعل ( فشيء منه ) أي من الكمال الأول الذي هو الحركة (بعد بالقوة فهو أي ذلك الكمال الأول إنما يثبت ( لما هو بالقوة ) من وجهين : أحدهما ذلك الكمال الثاني المترقب حال الحركة، وثانيهما نفس هذا الكمال الأول وتوضيحه يجب
قوله : ( في مكانه أو على حاله الأول في الحركة الأينية والثاني في غيرها . قوله : ( وقد يعتبر في مفهوم إلى آخره كما في تعريف اللذة بأنه إدراك ونيل بما هو كمال وخير عند المدرك إذا حصل بالفعل لأن الكمال لا يطلق إلا بعد الحصول . قوله : ( وتوضيحه إلخ زاد في التوضيح تصوير الكمال الأول والثاني في صورة جزئية وبيان كونه تعريفاً للحركة بالخاصة وبيان احترازات القيد . الموجود فيه بالنسبة إلى المقبول بالفعل، فهو من الأمور الحقيقية بخلاف الفعل، وأما ثانياً فلان الإضافات لما جوز أن تكون للعقول بالقوة، لم يظهر بمجرد ما ذكره من كونها بالفعل في الصفات الغير الإضافية استحالة الحركة عليها، فتأمل. بالفعل. قوله : ( أي الموصوف بالحركة فسر المتحرك به حذراً عن اللغوية في قوله : له حركة قوله : (أي معنى الكمال هو الحاصل بالفعل فيه بحث لأنَّ التعريف متناول لهيوليات الأجسام وليست عندهم كمالات قطعاً، بخلاف صورها الجسمية والنوعية، نعم تصور سبق القوة الجسمية والنوعية أسهل ولو بالنسبة إلى نوعهما بخلاف تصوره بالنظر إلى الهيولى وخروجها عن التعريف بهذا القدر غير واضح.
Then, that it (i.e., the moving thing, and it is necessary) as long as it is actually moving, something of it (i.e., of the first perfection which is motion) remains in potentiality. So that first perfection is only established for that which is in potentiality from two aspects: one is that second perfection anticipated during motion, and the other is this very first perfection. And its clarification is necessary. His statement: (in its place or its state: the first in local motion and the second in other motions). His statement: (and consideration of suitability in the concept etc.) as in the definition of pleasure as an apprehension and attainment of what is perfection and good for the perceiver when actually attained, because perfection is only used after attainment. His statement: (and its clarification etc.) he added in clarification a depiction of the first and second perfection in a particular instance and an explanation that it is a definition of motion by its specific property and an explanation of the precautions of the restriction. What exists in it relative to what is actually received, so it is from real matters unlike the act. And secondly, because relations, since they are allowed to be potential for the intellects, it does not appear merely from what he mentioned of their being actual in non-relational attributes that motion is impossible for them, so reflect. (bi al-fi'l). His statement: (i.e., the thing described by motion) he interpreted 'the moving thing' by it to avoid the triviality in his saying: 'it has motion.' His statement: (i.e., the meaning of perfection is the actually attained) there is discussion in it because the definition includes the hylomorphic matters of bodies, and they are certainly not perfections according to them, unlike their corporeal and specific forms. Yes, conceptualizing the priority of potentiality of the corporeal and specific forms is easier, even relative to their species, unlike conceptualizing it with respect to the hylic matter, and its exclusion from the definition to this extent is not clear.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage first states a necessary condition: as long as the moving thing is actually moving, part of the first perfection (motion) remains in potentiality. This potentiality is from two aspects: (1) the second perfection (the endpoint) anticipated during motion, and (2) the first perfection itself (the motion continuing). Then the commentary explains various points from the previous base text. It clarifies that 'in its place' refers to local motion, and 'in its state' to other motions. It discusses the concept of suitability (liyaq) in perfection, using pleasure as an example. The commentary also notes that the definition of motion as a perfection might inadvertently include hylomorphic matter (hayula), which is not considered a perfection by the philosophers. It suggests that the definition might be intended only for corporeal and specific forms, but it is unclear how to exclude hylic matter. The commentary ends by urging reflection.

Key Terms
بالقوة (bi al-quwwa)
الكمال الأول (al-kamal al-awwal)
الكمال الثاني (al-kamal al-thani)
الهيولى (al-hayula)
الصور الجسمية (al-suwar al-jismiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion is fully actual at every moment.
Correction: The passage shows that during motion, part of the first perfection (motion) remains in potentiality, meaning motion is never fully actual; it always has a potential component.

Checkpoint
From how many aspects does the first perfection remain in potentiality during motion?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
During motion, the first perfection (motion itself) is not fully actual; it retains potentiality from the anticipated endpoint and from its own continuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your spiritual growth (your 'motion' toward Allah), do you treat it as something that must remain partially potential, or do you expect instant perfection? How does the idea that even motion itself retains potentiality challenge your impatience with gradual change?
?
Everything in motion contains potentiality—a mark of createdness. In what areas of your life are you trying to be 'pure actuality,' relying only on your own plans and efforts? How can you instead embrace your dependence on the One who alone is without potentiality?
?
Consider a goal you are pursuing (a 'second perfection'). Do you despise the 'first perfection' of the journey itself, forgetting that the very movement carries potentiality that Allah sustains at each moment? What would it mean for you to truly value the process, not just the outcome?

Motion as a Perfection Achieved After Stability
Defines motion as an occurring affair after stability and explains perfection as actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of motion as an affair that occurs after a state of stability and why it is called a perfection for the moving body.

Core Idea
Motion is a perfection because it is actuality after potentiality.

In this passage, the author defines motion as something that occurs to a body after it was not present when the body was at rest. This 'something' is then identified as a perfection (كمال) for the mover. Let us examine the precise definition and the reasoning behind calling motion a perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of motion
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how motion is defined relative to a state of stability in place or state.
2
Identify the perfection claim
The text states that the occurring affair is a perfection for the mover. What does perfection mean here?
3
Understand the two conditions
Note the phrase 'in its place or in its state' – the commentator explains these refer to locative and non-locative motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتصافه بها (وهو) أي الحركة ( أمر حصل له بعد أن لم يكن حاصلاً له عند استقراره في مكانه أو على حاله ) فهو ( أي ذلك الأمر الحاصل بعد ما لم يكن ( كمال له ) أي للمتحرك (إذ معنى الكمال ذلك ) هذا إشارة إلى المطلق المذكور في ضمن المقيد أي معنى الكمال هو الحاصل بالفعل سواء كان مسبوقاً بالقوة، كما في حركات الحيوانات أو غير مسبوق بها كما في الكمالات الدائمة الحصول، والحركات الأزلية على رأي الفلاسفة، وإنما سمي الحاصل بالفعل كمالاً ، لأنَّ في القوة نقصاناً والفعل تام بالقياس إليها، وهذه التسمية لا تقتضي سبق القوة بل يكفيها تصورها وفرضها وقد يعتبر في مفهوم الكمال كونه لائقاً بما حصل فيه، لكنه ليس بمعتبر هاهنا إذ لا أن تكون الحركة لائقة بصاحبها. قوله : ( في مكانه أو على حاله الأول في الحركة الأينية والثاني في غيرها . قوله : ( وقد يعتبر في مفهوم إلى آخره كما في تعريف اللذة بأنه إدراك ونيل بما هو كمال وخير عند المدرك إذا حصل بالفعل لأن الكمال لا يطلق إلا بعد الحصول . الموجود فيه بالنسبة إلى المقبول بالفعل، فهو من الأمور الحقيقية بخلاف الفعل، وأما ثانياً فلان الإضافات لما جوز أن تكون للعقول بالقوة، لم يظهر بمجرد ما ذكره من كونها بالفعل في الصفات الغير الإضافية استحالة الحركة عليها، فتأمل. بالفعل. قوله : ( أي الموصوف بالحركة فسر المتحرك به حذراً عن اللغوية في قوله : له حركة قوله : (أي معنى الكمال هو الحاصل بالفعل فيه بحث لأنَّ التعريف متناول لهيوليات الأجسام وليست عندهم كمالات قطعاً، بخلاف صورها الجسمية والنوعية، نعم تصور سبق القوة الجسمية والنوعية أسهل ولو بالنسبة إلى نوعهما بخلاف تصوره بالنظر إلى الهيولى وخروجها عن التعريف بهذا القدر غير واضح.
Its description with it: (and it) meaning motion (is an affair that occurs to it after not having been present for it when it is settled in its place or in its state). So it (i.e., that affair that occurs after it was not) (is a perfection for it) i.e., for the mover (since the meaning of perfection is that). This refers to the absolute mentioned within the restricted, i.e., the meaning of perfection is the actually existent, whether it is preceded by potentiality, as in the movements of animals, or not preceded by it, as in the perfections that are perpetual in existence, and the eternal movements according to the philosophers. And the actually existent is called perfection only because in potentiality there is deficiency and actuality is complete relative to it. This naming does not require the precedence of potentiality; rather its conception and supposition suffice. And it may be considered in the concept of perfection its being suitable to what it occurs in, but it is not considered here, since [it is not] that motion is suitable to its possessor. He said: (in its place or in its state) the first for locative motion and the second for non-locative. He said: (and it may be considered in the concept... etc.) as in the definition of pleasure as a perception and attainment of that which is a perfection and good for the perceiver when it is actually attained, because perfection is not applied except after attainment. What exists in it relative to the recipient is actual, so it is among real things unlike the act. Secondly, since relations might be intellects with potentiality, it does not appear merely from his statement that they are actual in non-relative attributes the impossibility of motion upon them, so reflect. He said: (meaning the one described with motion) he interpreted 'mover' by it to avoid linguistic redundancy in his statement: 'it has motion'. He said: (meaning the meaning of perfection is the actually existent) in it there is a discussion because the definition includes the hylê of bodies, and according to them they are certainly not perfections, unlike their corporeal and specific forms. Yes, conceiving the precedence of potentiality for corporeal and specific forms is easier, even relative to their kind, unlike conceiving it with respect to hylê, and its exit from the definition to this extent is not clear.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage defines motion and explains the concept of perfection. It begins with the definition of motion as something that occurs to a body after not being present when the body was stable in its place or state. Then it states that this something is a perfection (كمال) for the mover. The commentator explains that perfection means the actually existent (الحاصل بالفعل), whether or not it was preceded by potentiality. Examples are given: animal movements are preceded by potentiality, while eternal movements (according to philosophers) are not. The term 'perfection' is applied to actuality because potentiality is a deficiency and actuality is complete relative to it. Importantly, this naming does not require actual prior potentiality; it suffices to conceive or suppose potentiality. The passage also notes that perfection may be considered as being suitable to its subject, but that consideration is not relevant here. The commentary then clarifies the types of motion (locative and qualitative) and discusses the definition of perfection further, including an example from the definition of pleasure. It also raises a discussion about whether the definition includes prime matter (هيولى) and argues that prime matter is not a perfection.

فعل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perfection always requires prior potentiality.
Correction: The text states that perfection is the actually existent, regardless of whether it was preceded by potentiality or not. The term 'perfection' is applied to actuality because potentiality is deficiency, but prior potentiality is not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'كمال' (perfection)?
A) Something preceded by potentiality
B) Something actual, whether or not preceded by potentiality
C) Something suitable to its subject
D) Eternal movement

Key Takeaway
Motion is a perfection because it is actuality, and perfection means the actually existent, regardless of prior potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage teaches that perfection does not require a prior deficiency; you can be doing good deeds that are perfect simply because they are actual. Is there a good deed you keep postponing because you feel unprepared? Will you perform it right now, trusting that the actuality itself is perfection?
?
You have been given the potential for iman, salat, and charity, but are they still potential? What is one actual movement toward Allah that you will commit to before you sleep tonight, so that today is not a day of stability but of realizing your perfection?

Further Considerations on Perfection: Suitability and Prime Matter
Discusses the suitability condition and the objection about prime matter not being a perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional condition of suitability in the concept of perfection and the philosophical discussion about whether prime matter can be considered a perfection.

Core Idea
Perfections must be suitable to their subject; prime matter is not a perfection.

The author continues by noting that perfection sometimes includes the condition of being suitable (لائق) to the thing it occurs in, though this condition is not relevant for motion. Then the commentator raises a critical discussion: the definition of perfection as the actually existent includes prime matter (هيولى), which philosophers do not consider a perfection. This lesson unpacks these subtle points.

Guided Path
1
Read the suitability condition
The passage mentions that perfection may be considered as being suitable to what it occurs in, but it is not considered here. Why?
Because motion may not be suitable to its possessor in the same way.
2
Understand the objection about prime matter
The commentator notes that the definition of perfection as the actually existent would include prime matter (هيولى), which is not a perfection. Why is prime matter not a perfection?
3
Reflect on the resolution
The passage concludes that conceiving potentiality for prime matter is harder, and it is not clear how to exclude it.
This shows a nuance in the definition.

Motion as Traversal: A Mental Construct
Sanusi defines the second meaning of motion and proves it cannot exist externally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second meaning of motion as traversal and why it cannot exist in external reality.

Core Idea
Motion as traversal exists only in the mind, not in the physical world.

Sanusi now takes up a subtle but crucial distinction: motion can be understood not just as a process but as a complete traversal from start to end. This 'motion as traversal' seems plausible, but upon analysis, it has no external existence. In this passage, he defines it and proves its impossibility in external objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the definition of the second meaning of motion carefully. Note that it is 'the thing from the beginning of the distance to its end' and is called 'motion in the sense of traversal'.
2
Identify the claim of impossibility
Sanusi states that this motion exists only in the imagination (التوهم) and not in external entities. Ask yourself why that might be.
3
Follow the first proof
The proof focuses on the moving body's relation to the parts of the distance. When the body reaches the second part, its relation to the first part has ceased. Therefore, there is no extended whole from start to end at any moment.
4
Understand the alternative formulation
Sanusi restates the proof: before reaching the endpoint, the motion is incomplete; after reaching, the motion has ceased. So at no moment does the complete traversal exist externally.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Contemplate why this matters: it shows that 'motion as traversal' is a mental construct, not a real entity in the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعنى (الثاني) للحركة هو ( الأمر من أول المسافة إلى آخرها وهو الحركة بمعنى القطع ) ولا وجود لها إلا في التوهم ( لاستحالة وجودها في الأعيان ) إذ عند الحصول في الجزء الثاني من بطل نسبته ( أي نسبة المتحرك ) إلى الجزء الأول منها ضرورة فلا يوجد هناك أمر ممتد من مبدئها إلى منتهاها، وبعبارة أخرى المتحرك ما لم يصل إلى المنتهى لم توجد الحركة بتمامها، وإذا وصل فقد انقطعت الحركة فلا وجود لها في الخارج أصلاً
The second meaning of motion is: the thing from the beginning of the distance to its end, and it is motion in the sense of traversal. And it has no existence except in imagination, because its existence in external entities is impossible. For when [the moving body] is in the second part, its relation (i.e., the relation of the moving body) to the first part has necessarily ceased, so there is no extended thing from its beginning to its end. In other words: as long as the moving body has not reached the endpoint, the motion in its entirety does not exist; and when it has reached, the motion has ceased, so it has no existence in external reality at all.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines the second meaning of motion (الحركة بمعنى القطع) as motion considered as the continuous stretch from the start to the end of the distance. The author states that such motion exists only in the imagination (التوهم), not in the external world. The proof given is that when the moving object arrives at the second part of the distance, its relation to the first part has necessarily vanished; therefore, there is no extended entity spanning from beginning to end. Another formulation: before reaching the endpoint, the motion is incomplete; upon reaching it, the motion is over, so at no moment does the complete motion exist externally.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى القطع
التوهم
المسافة
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion as a whole exists at each moment as a single entity.
Correction: This passage shows that motion as traversal is a gradual entity that cannot be present all at once; it exists only in the mind as a complete whole.

Checkpoint
Why does the second meaning of motion (motion as traversal) have no existence in external reality?
A) Because motion is a mental concept only.
B) Because when the moving body is in the second part, its relation to the first part has ceased, so there is no extended whole.
C) Because motion is gradual and cannot be perceived.
D) Because the distance is composed of indivisible parts.

Key Takeaway
The second meaning of motion (motion as traversal) is a mental construct that cannot be realized in external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson says that the continuous motion you perceive from start to finish is actually a mental construct. Are you willing to examine the deepest foundations of your perception of change and time? What assumptions about reality might you be holding that are only in your imagination? Commit to questioning one such assumption today.
?
If motion as a whole does not exist externally, then everything you experience as a smooth flow—your life's journey, your actions—is a mental representation. How does this affect your understanding of your own existence and your relationship with the Creator who sustains each moment? What changes must you make in your life as a result?

Why Gradual Existence is Impossible
The author refutes an objection by proving gradual existence is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single thing cannot come into existence gradually and how this refutes the objector's claim about motion.

Core Idea
Gradual existence of a single thing is impossible because each moment brings a distinct entity, not a continuous whole.

The issue of motion raises a deep question: Is motion a single continuous thing or a succession of discrete states? An objector argues that when motion reaches its endpoint, the motion has existed throughout the entire time. The author responds by showing that the very idea of gradual existence for a single thing is unintelligible. Let's examine this critical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: a conditional objection and the author's response beginning with 'I say'.
The objection is introduced by 'If you say' and the answer by 'I say'.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the premise that 'what comes to be in the first part of time must be different from what comes to be in the second part.' Understand why the author says this is necessary.
The reason is that an existent cannot be identical to a non-existent.
3
Understand the conclusion about gradualism
Realize that if each part yields a different entity, then there is no single thing coming to be gradually. Instead, there are multiple distinct entities each appearing instantaneously.
This refutes the idea of motion as a single gradual process.
4
Apply the argument to motion
See how this leads to the conclusion that motion as a continuous traversal (al-qat') does not exist externally. The gloss extends the argument to the first meaning of motion as well.
The commentator explains how the objection applies with a slight modification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: إذا وصل إلى المنتهى فالحركة اتصفت حال الوصول بأنها وجدت في جميع ذلك الزمان، لا في شيء من أجزائه، قلت: حصول الشيء الواحد في نفسه على سبيل التدريج غير معقول، لأنَّ الحاصل في الجزء الأول من الزمان لا بد أن يكون مغايراً لما يحصل في الجزء الثاني لامتناع أن يكون الموجود عين المعدوم، فيكون هناك أشياء متغايرة متعاقبة لا يتصل بعضها ببعض اتصالاً حقيقياً لامتناع أن يتصل المعدوم بالموجود كذلك ويكون كل واحد منها حاصلاً دفعة لا تدريجاً، فلا وجود للحركة بمعنى القطع في الخارج. قوله : ( فإن قلت إلخ ) هذا السؤال وارد على الوجه الأول أيضاً بأدنى تغير بأن يقال : اللازم ذلك الوجه أن لا تكون موجودة بين الحصول في الحيز الأول والثاني، لا أن تكون موجودة أصلاً لجواز واحد بالاعتبار فإنه باعتبار حصوله منه في زمان واحد آخر جزء منه بالتدريج وإن كان في الحقيقة حصول أشياء متعددة .
If you say: 'When it reaches the endpoint, motion is characterized at the moment of arrival as having existed in all that time, not in any of its parts,' I say: 'The coming-to-be of a single thing in itself in a gradual manner is unintelligible, because what comes to be in the first part of time must be different from what comes to be in the second part, since it is impossible for the existent to be identical with the non-existent. So there are multiple different successive things, none of which is truly connected to another, because it is impossible for the non-existent to be thus connected to the existent. And each of them comes to be instantaneously, not gradually. Thus motion in the sense of traversal has no existence externally.' His saying: '(If you say, etc.)': This objection applies to the first meaning as well, with a slight modification: it would entail that motion does not exist between being in the first place and the second, not that it does not exist at all, because it is possible to consider it as one thing in terms of its coming to be from it in one time, another part of it gradually, even though in reality it is the obtaining of multiple things.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector claims that when the moving object reaches the endpoint, the motion can be described as having existed throughout the entire time, not in any single part. The author answers that a single thing cannot come into existence gradually: what appears in the first part of time must be different from what appears in the second part, since an existent cannot be identical to a non-existent. Thus we have a succession of distinct, unconnected things, each coming into existence instantaneously, not gradually. Therefore, motion as a continuous whole does not exist externally. The commentator notes that this objection also applies to the first meaning of motion (motion as intermediate state), but with a modification: it would show that motion does not exist between the two positions, not that it never exists at all, because it can be considered one thing gradually obtained in time, though in reality it consists of multiple entities.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion as a gradual process can be a single continuous entity.
Correction: Gradual existence of a single thing is impossible; each moment brings a distinct entity, so motion is a succession of discrete states.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the objection, why is the gradual coming-to-be of a single thing impossible?
A) Because time is indivisible.
B) Because what exists in the first part of time must be different from what exists in the second, and an existent cannot become non-existent gradually.
C) Because motion is only in the mind.
D) Because the objector's premise is false.

Key Takeaway
Gradual existence of a single thing is impossible, so motion cannot be a single continuous entity in the external world; it is a succession of instantaneous discrete states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

But this lesson shows that each moment is a separate existence, disconnected from the next. If every action you take is a series of discrete instants, not a continuous whole, then how can you claim responsibility for a 'single' sin or a 'single' good deed? Are you willing to face the fact that your identity is not a continuous self but a sequence of distinct snapshots, each a new creation?
?
Consider your daily prayers: you move from standing to bowing to prostrating. If motion is not one continuous thing, then each posture is a separate act. How does that change your awareness of each moment of prayer? Can you truly say you performed 'one' rak'ah, or is it a series of separate events that Allah recreates each instant?
?
The objector thought that motion exists in the whole time but not in its parts—a comforting illusion of continuity. What comfort do you rely on that might be an illusion? What part of your life have you been treating as a 'gradual existence' that is actually a collection of atomic moments, each demanding its own intention and accountability?

Existence of Motion in the Mind and Two Ways of Imagining It
Explains mental existence of motion and two ways of imagining it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the meaning of motion as traversal exists in the mind through the retention of successive images, and the two ways scholars describe to conceive the resulting extended thing.

Core Idea
Motion in the mind is constructed from overlapping images, not directly perceived.

The author has established that motion in the sense of traversal cannot exist externally. Now he explains how it does exist in the mind. The mechanism involves imprinting the moving body's relation to successive positions before the earlier image fades. A commentator adds two ways to conceive the resulting extended thing: by connection of images like merging water, or by their simultaneous presence preparing the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the main passage carefully
Start with the author's own words: 'Yes, it has existence in the mind...' and understand how the imprinting of relations creates an extended image in the imagination.
Notice the key term 'irtisām' (imprinting).
2
Study the two ways from the gloss
After the main passage, the commentary provides two ways to conceive the extended thing. Read them and contrast the metaphors: merging water vs. simultaneous presence preparing the mind.
These are two different explanations for the same mental phenomenon.
3
Reflect on the mental existence
Ask yourself: Why is this mental construction necessary? How does it differ from external motion? This shows the subtlety of kalam reasoning about attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم لها وجود في الذهن فإنه لما ارتسم نسبته ( أي نسبة المتحرك ) إلى الجزء الثاني الذي أدركه في الخيال قبل أن تزول نسبته إلى الجزء الأول الذي ترك عنه ( أي عن الخيال ) يتخيل أمر ممتد يعني أن للمتحرك نسبة إلى المكان الذي تحركه ونسبة إلى المكان الذي أدركه، فإذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة كونه في المكان الأول، وقبل زوالها عن الخيال ارتسمت فيه صورة كونه في المكان الثاني . قوله : ( فإنه لما ارتسم نسبته إلخ ) قال الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين، يتصور حصول أمر ممتد من أول المسافة إلى آخرها في الذهن بوجهين أحدهما، أن يقال : إن إحدى الصورتين اتصلت بالأخرى فيحصل أمر ممتد منهما يشبه اتصال الماء بالماء، وصيرورتهما أمراً ممتداً واحداً والثاني أن يقال : حصولهما معاً صار معداً للذهن لحصول أمر ممتد فيه
Yes, it has existence in the mind. For when the relation of the moving body to the second part, which it has perceived in the imagination, becomes imprinted before its relation to the first part, which it has left, vanishes from the imagination, an extended thing is imagined. That is: the moving body has a relation to the place from which it moved and a relation to the place it has perceived. So when the image of its being in the first place is imprinted in the imagination, and before that image vanishes from the imagination, the image of its being in the second place is imprinted. His saying: 'For when its relation becomes imprinted, etc.' The commentator said in the glosses on Hikmat al-'Ayn: The obtaining of an extended thing from the beginning of the distance to its end in the mind is conceived in two ways. One: one of the two images connects with the other, so an extended thing results from them, resembling the connection of water with water and their becoming one extended thing. The second: their simultaneous obtaining prepares the mind for the obtaining of an extended thing in it.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage explains how motion in the sense of traversal, which cannot exist externally, does exist in the mind. The mechanism is that the relation of the moving body to the second part (the place it has reached) is imprinted in the imagination before the relation to the first part (the place it left) has faded away. Thus, the mind imagines an extended thing running from the first to the second. The commentator adds two ways from the gloss on Hikmat al-'Ayn: (1) the two images (of the first and second positions) become connected like water merging into one continuous thing, or (2) the simultaneous presence of both images prepares the mind to conceive an extended thing. Note that this is a mental, not external, existence.

Misconception: One might think the mind directly perceives motion as a single continuous entity.
Correction: The passage shows that motion in the mind is constructed from successive images retained together; it is not directly perceived as an extended whole.

Checkpoint

A) It exists externally and is perceived directly.
B) Through the imprinting of successive relations before the earlier one fades.
C) Only through intellectual abstraction without images.
D) By the continuous movement of the imagination itself.

Key Takeaway
Motion (traversal) exists mentally by retaining overlapping images of successive positions, and scholars explain this through two models: connection of images or their simultaneous presence preparing the mind.

The First View: Motion as Change in the Category Itself
Presentation and refutation of the first view on motion in categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of motion occurring in a category and its refutation through the example of blackening.

Core Idea
Motion in a category cannot mean the category itself changes; change is in attributes, not essence.

The author begins by presenting the first of two views on how motion occurs in a category. According to this view, the category itself remains identical while gradually changing, making it the real subject of motion. This seems plausible, but the author offers a powerful counterexample to show why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the first view definition
Read the Arabic passage describing the first view: motion occurs when the category changes gradually while remaining identical.
Notice the phrase 'مع بقائها بعينها' — the category is said to remain the same.
2
Examine the blackening example
The author uses the example of blackening (التسود) to test the view. Consider the three possibilities he outlines.
The example is about the essence of blackness, not a physical object.
3
Understand the refutation logic
The refutation shows that in all cases, the change is not in the essence of blackness but in an additional quality, contradicting the view.
This demonstrates why the category cannot be the real subject of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها ] ( فيما يقع فيه الحركة من المقولات عندهم ذهب جماعة إلى أن معنى وقوع الحركة في مقولة هو أن تلك المقولة مع بقائها بعينها تتغير من حال إلى حال على سبيل التدريج، فتكون تلك المقولة هي الموضوع الحقيقي لتلك الحركة، سواء قلنا : إِنَّ الجوهر الذي هو موضوع تلك المقولة موصوف بتلك الحركة بالعرض، وعلى سبيل التبع أو لم نقل وهو باطل لأن التسود مثلا ليس هو أن ذات السواد يشتد، ذلك السواد إن عدم عند الاشتداد، فليس فيه اشتداد قطعاً، وإن بقي ولم تحدث فيه صفة زائدة، فلا اشتداد فيه أيضاً وإن حدثت فيه صفة زائدة فلا تبدل ولا اشتداد ولا حركة ذات السواد، بل في صفته والمفروض خلافه، وذهب آخرون إلى أن معنى وقوعها في أن تلك المقولة جنس لتلك الحركة قالوا : إن من في مقولة هو الأين، ما الأين ما هو قادر ومنه ما هو هذه الأجناس سيال وكذا الحال في الكم والكيف والوضع فالسيال من كل جنس من هو الحركة فتكون الحركة نوعاً من ذلك الجنس، وهو أيضاً باطل إذ لا معنى للحركة إلا تغير الموضوع في صفاته على سبيل التدريج ولا شك أن التغير ليس من جنس المتغير والمتبدل لأنَّ التبدل حالة نسبية إضافية والمتبدل ليس كذلك، فإذا كان المتبدل في الحركة هذه المقولات لم يكن شيء منها جنساً للتبدل الواقع فيها.
[The Third Intent: The occurrence of motion among categories and its changes] (Concerning what motion occurs in among the categories according to them: A group went to that the meaning of the occurrence of motion in a category is that that category, while remaining identical, changes from state to state gradually, so that category is the real subject of that motion, whether we say that the substance which is the subject of that category is described by that motion accidentally and dependently, or we do not say it. And it is false because blackening, for example, is not that the essence of blackness intensifies; that blackness if it perishes upon intensification, then there is no intensification in it absolutely, and if it remains and no additional quality occurs in it, then there is no intensification in it either; and if an additional quality occurs in it, then there is no change, no intensification, and no motion of the essence of blackness, but rather in its quality, and the presupposed is contrary to that. And others went to that the meaning of its occurrence is that that category is a genus for that motion. They said: among what is in a category is 'where' (al-ayn); some 'where' is potential and some of it is these flowing genera; and likewise the case in quantity, quality, and posture; thus the flowing from each genus is motion, so motion is a species of that genus. And it is also false, for there is no meaning of motion except the change of the subject in its attributes gradually, and there is no doubt that change is not of the genus of the changing and the altered, because alteration is a relative relational state, and the altered is not so. So if the altered in motion is these categories, none of them is a genus for the change occurring in them.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents two views on the meaning of motion occurring in a category and refutes both. The author begins by stating the first view: some scholars hold that motion occurs in a category when that category itself remains identical while gradually changing from one state to another, making the category the real subject of motion. The author refutes this using the example of blackening (التسود). If the essence of blackness intensifies, it either perishes (so no intensification), remains without an additional quality (no intensification), or an additional quality occurs (then the change is in the quality, not in the essence of blackness, which contradicts the presupposed). The second view holds that the category is a genus for motion; for example, 'where' (الأين) includes both static and flowing (سيال) types, and similarly for other categories, so motion is a species of that genus. The author refutes this by noting that motion is change, and change is not of the same genus as the thing that changes, because change is a relational state. Therefore, none of the categories can be a genus for the change that occurs in them.

Key Terms

الحركة
الموضوع الحقيقي
التسود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion in a category means the category itself changes while remaining identical.
Correction: The category does not change; only attributes (qualities) change. The example of blackening shows that any change is in a quality, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, what does the example of blackening demonstrate?
A) The essence of blackness intensifies.
B) The change occurs in a quality, not the essence.
C) The category itself changes gradually.
D) Motion is impossible in categories.

Key Takeaway
The first view fails because change must be in an attribute, not the essence of the category itself.

The Second View: Motion as a Species of the Category Genus
Presentation and refutation of the second view on motion in categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of motion occurring in a category as a species of a genus and its refutation based on the nature of change.

Core Idea
Change (motion) is a relational state, not a species of the category it occurs in.

The second view proposes that motion occurs in a category by being a species of that category, treating the category as a genus that includes both static and flowing instances. The author refutes this by clarifying that change is not of the same genus as the thing that changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the second view definition
Read the Arabic passage describing the view: motion is a species of the category because the category contains both static and flowing types.
Notice the example of 'where' (الأين) having both static and flowing instances.
2
Understand the refutation
The author argues that motion is change, and change is a relational state, not from the genus of the thing that changes.
This is a logical argument: the genus of a thing cannot be the same as the genus of its change.
3
Apply the refutation to categories
Since the altered in motion are categories, none of them can be a genus for the change that occurs in them.
This refutes the view that motion is a species of the category.

5 min
Refuting the Genus View of Motion in Categories
Refutes the claim that a category is a genus for motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion cannot be a species under a category and how the refutation relies on the definition of motion as gradual change.

Core Idea
Motion is change, not a genus — categories are not genera for motion.

Before presenting the correct view, the author first refutes a common misconception: that motion belongs to a category as a species under a genus. He argues that change is a relational state, not a substance, so it cannot be classified under the categories that describe substances. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false view
Read the passage to see what others claimed about motion and categories: they held that the category is a genus for motion, making motion a species under that genus.
The false view is presented as the opinion of 'others'.
2
Understand the refutation
Notice how the author defines motion as gradual change in the subject's attributes and argues that change is not of the same genus as the thing changed. Change is a relational, additive state.
The key insight: change is relative, not substantial.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The false view is invalid because the categories cannot serve as genera for motion. Since change is not a substance, it cannot fall under a categorical genus.
This sets up the correct definition in the next lesson.

قوله : ( لأن ذلك السواد إلخ) أي السواد الذي فرض متحركاً سواء كان الحركة من نوع إلى نوع أو من صنف إلى صنف أو من فرد إلى فرد وما قيل إن جنس السواد باق هو يشتد والمعدوم نوعه أو النوع باق وهو يشتد والمعدوم فرده فوهم لامتناع بقاء حصة الجنس أو النوع مع انعدام الفرد.
قوله : (بل صفته إلخ) أي بل التبدل في صفته بأن حدثت بعد ما لم تكن حادثة فيه أوجدت صفة ما لم يكن ليس حركة .
قوله : ( أنَّ تلك المقولة فانظر فيه ظرفية العام للخاص.
قوله : ( إنَّ الموضوع إلخ ) فالمقولة مسافة الحركة وهو الظاهر من الظرفية .
كما يتبادر من قوله : بعينها وقوله : متغير من حال إلى حال إذ لا شك في لزوم بقاء الموضوع بعينه الحالين في جميع الحركات، فبقاء جنس الموضوع وتبدل أنواعه مناف له قطعاً. قوله : ( والمفروض خلافه قيل لا نسلم لزوم خلاف المفروض إذ معنى الحركة في المقولة على الفرض المذكور أن يكون نفس المقولة باقية بعينها ومتغيرة من حال إلى حال، وهذا المعنى متحقق في الصورة الثالثة فليس فيها خلاف المفروض وجوابه أن المراد من المفروض هو الحركة في الكيف إذ معناه المتبادر منه على قياس الحركة في الأين هو الانتقال من كيف إلى كيف فبقاء الكيف وتغير حاله ينافي هذا الغرض
قوله: (والصواب أن معنى وقوعها إلخ ) سيأتي تحقيق هذا في بحث التركة في الأين وسنتكلم عليه هناك إن شاء الله تعالى .
Others held that the meaning of its occurrence [in a category] is that that category is a genus for that motion. They said: Whatever is in a category is [؟] the 'where' (al-ayn) what what the 'where' what is able? and from it these fluid genera, and similarly for quantity, quality, and position, so the fluid from each genus is motion, so motion is a species of that genus. It is also false because motion means nothing but the subject's change in its attributes in gradual fashion, and there is no doubt that change is not of the genus of the changing and the changed, because change is a relative additive state, while the changed is not so. So if the changed in motion were these categories, none of them would be a genus for the change occurring in them. The correct view is that the meaning of its occurrence in them is that the subject moves from a species of that category to another species of it, or from a class to a class, or from an individual to an individual. (And they (i.e., the categories in which motion occurs) are four, as is well-known: the first is quantity, and its occurrence in it is in four aspects, because motion in quantity is either by increase or by decrease.
His statement: '(Because that blackness, etc.)' means the blackness which was assumed to be moving, whether the motion is from species to species or from class to class or from individual to individual. And what was said: the genus of blackness remains and it intensifies while its species ceases, or the species remains and it intensifies while its individual ceases, is an illusion because it is impossible for a portion of the genus or species to remain with the cessation of the individual.
His statement: '(But its attribute, etc.)' means rather the change in its attribute, that it occurred after not having occurred in it; an attribute was brought about that was not there; it is not motion.
His statement: '(That category, consider in it)' is the containment of the general for the specific.
His statement: '(The subject, etc.)' so the category is the distance of motion, and that is apparent from containment.
As understood from his statement: 'itself' and his statement: 'changing from state to state', since there is no doubt that the subject itself must remain in both states in all motions. So the preservation of the genus of the subject and the change of its species is certainly incompatible with this. His statement: '(And the assumption contradicts it).' It was said: We do not accept that it contradicts the assumption, because the meaning of motion in the category according to the mentioned assumption is that the category itself remains and changes from state to state, and this meaning is realized in the third form, so there is no contradiction of the assumption in it. The answer is that what is meant by the assumption is motion in quality, because its understood meaning by analogy with motion in 'where' is transition from quality to quality. So the preservation of quality and change of its state contradicts this purpose.
His statement: '(And the correct view that the meaning of its occurrence, etc.)' The verification of this will come in the discussion of the 'tarka' (inheritance?) in 'where', and we will discuss it there, God willing.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific view about the occurrence of motion in categories and states the correct definition. It begins by presenting the position of some scholars who hold that when motion occurs in a category, that category serves as a genus for motion. They illustrate by saying that genera like 'where', quantity, quality, and position are fluid (sayyāl) in the sense that they contain motion, and motion becomes a species under them. The commentator immediately declares this view false (bāṭil). The reason is that motion is defined as the gradual change of the subject in its attributes. Change is not of the same genus as the thing changed; change is a relational, additive state, while the changed thing is not. Therefore, if the changed thing in motion were these categories, none of them would be a genus for the change. The correct view (al-ṣawāb) is then stated: motion occurs when the subject moves from one species of that category to another species, or from one class to another, or from one individual to another. This is a definition focusing on the subject's transition within the category.

The passage then enumerates the four categories in which motion occurs, as is well-known: quantity, quality, position, and 'where'. It begins with quantity, stating that motion in quantity occurs in four aspects: increase and decrease, each further subdivided. The text then moves into a series of explanatory comments (qawluhu) that clarify earlier statements from the main text (al-matan). The first comment explains that when blackness is assumed to be moving, whether by species, class, or individual, the claim that the genus remains while the species ceases is impossible, as a portion of the genus cannot survive the disappearance of its individual. The second comment clarifies that change in an attribute occurring after non-existence is not motion. The third comment interprets the containment analogy ('containment of the general for the specific') as indicating that the category is the 'distance' (masāfa) of motion. The fourth comment emphasises that the subject itself must remain throughout motion, and preserving the genus while changing species contradicts this. Finally, a objection and response are given: some might argue that if the category itself remains and changes state, that fits a certain form, but the response stipulates that the assumption refers to motion in quality, which involves transition from one quality to another, so preserving the quality while changing its state negates the intended meaning. The passage concludes by deferring full verification to a later discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a species under a category and the category is its genus.
Correction: Motion is gradual change, which is a relational state, not a substance; hence it cannot be a species under a categorical genus.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for rejecting the genus view?
A) Motion is too fast to be classified.
B) Change is a relational state, not a substance, so it cannot be a species under a genus.
C) The categories are not fluid enough.
D) The subject does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Motion, as gradual change, is not a genus-able entity; categories are not its genera.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that change is not a genus — it is a relative state, not something you can be defined by. Where in your life have you attached your identity to a temporary state of change (e.g., a struggle, a feeling, a phase), treating it as a fixed category? What would change if you saw it as a passing state instead? Commit to one concrete way to stop labeling yourself by what is only a change.
?
The author says that the changed thing (the subject) is not the same as the change. Your soul is the subject; your habits, thoughts, and actions are changes. If you confuse your essence with these changes, how might that lead to despair or pride? Name one change you have been identifying with, and resolve to see it as a mere attribute, not your core.
?
Motion is gradual change. Consider your spiritual journey: are you expecting instant transformation, neglecting the gradual nature of change? How does this lesson correct impatience in your relationship with Allah? Write down one area where you need to embrace gradual improvement and ask Allah for steadfastness.

The Correct Meaning and Categories of Motion
States the correct definition of motion in categories and enumerates the four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of motion occurring in categories and learn the four categories where motion operates, with focus on quantity.

Core Idea
Motion is the subject's transition within a category from one species, class, or individual to another.

After refuting the genus view, the author states the correct meaning: motion is the subject's movement across species, classes, or individuals within a category. He then lists the four categories known to admit motion and begins with quantity, explaining its two main modes.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the correct definition
The passage defines motion as the subject's movement from one species of a category to another species, or from one class to another, or from one individual to another. Note the three levels of transition.
The subject remains the same across the change.
2
Learn the four categories
The author states the well-known four categories in which motion occurs: quantity, quality, position, and 'where' (place). Only quantity is elaborated here.
These are the categories that admit gradual change.
3
Focus on motion in quantity
Motion in quantity is said to occur in four aspects? Actually, the text says 'on four aspects' but only mentions two: increase (izdiyād) and decrease (intiqāṣ). The other two are presumably implied by the division into two pairs.
Increase and decrease are the primary modes.
