Three Types of Essential Motion and the Mistake About Natural Motion
Classifies essential motion into three types and corrects a mistake about natural motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of essential motion (forced, voluntary, natural) based on the principle of motion and perception, and why natural motion includes more than just ascent and descent.

Core Idea
Essential motion is classified by where its principle lies and whether perception accompanies it.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a foundational classification in Aristotelian physics: how motion originating from a thing is divided into three categories. The author not only defines each type but also corrects a common mistake that restricts natural motion to only upward and downward movement. Understanding this helps us see why motions like plant growth and the pulse are considered natural.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the classification depends on two factors: the location of the principle of motion and the presence of perception.
2
Identify the three types of motion
Find the three types: forced, voluntary, natural. For each, note where the principle of motion is and whether there is perception.
The passage defines each type in order: forced (principle in another), voluntary (principle in the thing with perception), natural (principle in the thing without perception).
3
Understand the correction about natural motion
The author criticizes those who limit natural motion to ascent and descent. Ask yourself: why is that a mistake? What examples does the author give to show that natural motion is broader?
The examples are plant motion and the pulse—both have an internal principle but no conscious perception.
4
Connect to earlier discussions
The passage references 'investigations of inclination (mayl)' for more detail. Consider how this classification relates to the concept of mayl and other physical principles.
This helps situate the passage within the broader text of Sharh al-Mawaqif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنقسم حركته إلى ثلاثة أقسام لأنه إما أن يكون مبدأ الحركة في غيره وهي الحركة القسرية أو يكون مبدأ الحركة فيه إما مع الشعور أي شعور مبدأ الحركة بتلك الحركة وهي الحركة الإرادية أو لا مع الشعور وهي الحركة الطبيعية وعلى هذا فالحركة النباتية طبيعية وكذا حركة النبض لأن مبدأ هاتين الحركتين موجود في المتحرك ولا شعور له بالحركة الصادرة عنه وقد أخطأ من جعل الحركة الطبيعية الصاعدة والهابطة أي حصرها فيهما إذ يخرج عنها حينئذ حركة النبض كما مر في مباحث الميل والحركة النباتية أو
And its motion is divided into three divisions: because either the principle of motion is in something else—that is forced motion (al-ḥaraka al-qasriyya), or the principle of motion is in it (the moving thing) either with perception (shuʿūr)—that is, the principle of motion perceives that motion—and that is voluntary motion (al-ḥaraka al-irādiyya), or without perception, and that is natural motion (al-ḥaraka al-ṭabīʿiyya). Upon this, plant motion is natural, and likewise the pulse, because the principle of both motions is present in the moving thing and it has no perception of the motion emanating from it. And he who confined natural motion to ascending and descending has made a mistake—i.e., limited it to them—because then the pulse would be excluded, as mentioned in the investigations of inclination (mayl) and plant motion, or ...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the threefold division of essential (self-originated) motion and corrects a common error. It states that essential motion is divided into three types depending on the location of the principle of motion (mabdaʾ al-ḥaraka) and the presence or absence of perception. First, forced motion (qasriyya): the principle is external to the moving object. Second, voluntary motion (irādiyya): the principle is internal and accompanied by perception (shuʿūr) of the motion. Third, natural motion (ṭabīʿiyya): the principle is internal but without perception. The author then applies this: plant growth and the pulse are natural because their principle is within the organism and there is no conscious awareness of the motion. The passage criticizes those who restrict natural motion only to ascent and descent (e.g., heavy bodies falling and light bodies rising), because that would exclude motions like the pulse and plant growth, which are also natural. The author refers to earlier discussions on inclination (mayl) for further detail. The phrase 'or' at the end may introduce an alternative, but the main point is the classification and correction.

Misconception: Natural motion is only the upward or downward movement of heavy/light bodies.
Correction: Natural motion includes any motion whose principle is within the moving thing without perception, such as plant growth and the pulse, not just ascent/descent.

Classification of Motion into Six Types
Al-Iji classifies motion into six types based on the source of the moving force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji classifies motion into six distinct types based on the source and nature of the moving force.

Core Idea
Motion is classified by external vs. internal force, simple vs. composite, and presence of will and consciousness.

Al-Iji begins by presenting a systematic classification of motion, a key concept in natural philosophy and theology. The classification proceeds by dichotomies: first, external vs. internal force; then, simple vs. composite motion; and finally, with or without will and consciousness. This passage provides a clear logical division that students of kalam must understand.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full list. Note the logical structure: the first split is external vs. internal force.
2
Trace the subdivisions for internal force
Within internal force, motion is either simple (uniform) or composite. Simple is further divided by will (celestial vs. natural). Composite is divided by source (animal force vs. plant), and animal force further by consciousness (voluntary vs. compulsory).
3
Verify all six types are distinct
Check that each type is defined without overlap: coercive, celestial, natural, voluntary animal, compulsory animal, plant.
The passage uses the term 'compulsory' (taskhīriyya) for involuntary animal motion like the pulse.
4
Reflect on the importance of classification
Consider why kalam needs this classification—it grounds discussions about divine action, causation, and natural processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : الحركة وقياسها بالسرعة والزمن إلى ستة أقسام لأن القوة المحركة إن كانت خارجة عن المتحرك فالحركة قسرية، وإن لم تكن خارجة عنه فإما أن تكون الحركة بسيطة أي على نهج واحد، وإما مركبة لا على نهج واحد، والبسيطة إما أن تكون بإرادة وهي الحركة الفلكية، أو لا بإرادة وهي الطبيعية، والمركبة إما أن يكون مصدرها القوة الحيوانية أولاً، والثانية الحركة النباتية والأولى إما أن تكون مع شعور بها وهي الحركة الإرادية الحيوانية أولاً مع شعور وهي الحركة التسخيرية كحركة النبض.
Fourth Observatory - Eleventh Objective: Motion and its measurement by speed and time. [It is divided] into six divisions because the moving force, if it is external to the moved object, then the motion is coercive; and if it is not external to it, then either the motion is simple i.e. on one course, or composite not on one course. The simple: either it is with will and that is celestial motion, or without will and that is natural motion. The composite: either its source is animal force or not; the second is plant motion; and the first is either with consciousness of it and that is voluntary animal motion, or without consciousness and that is compulsory motion like the pulse.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of motion into six types. The basis of division is the nature of the moving force. If the force is from outside the moving body, the motion is coercive (qasriyya). If it is from within, then it is either simple (basīṭa) meaning uniform in direction, or composite (murakkaba). Simple motion is either with will (irāda), which is celestial motion (falakiyya), or without will, which is natural motion (ṭabī‘iyya). Composite motion is either from an animal force (qūwa ḥayawāniyya) or not; if not, it is plant motion (nabātiyya). If from an animal force, it is either with consciousness (shu‘ūr) and that is voluntary animal motion (irādiyya ḥayawāniyya), or without consciousness and that is compulsory motion (taskhīriyya) like the pulse. Thus six exhaustive types are enumerated.

Key Terms
حركة قسرية
حركة فلكية
حركة طبيعية
حركة حيوانية إرادية
حركة تسخيرية
حركة نباتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple motion is only natural motion, and composite motion is only animal motion.
Correction: Simple motion includes both celestial (with will) and natural (without will); composite motion includes plant motion and two types of animal motion.

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji's classification of motion into six types provides a comprehensive framework based on internal/external force, simplicity/composition, will, and consciousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The celestial motion is said to be 'with will' but without the composite nature of human voluntary motion. This implies a different kind of will. Reflect on how your own will is intertwined with deliberation and choice. Is there an area of your life where you wish your will were more like the celestial—single-minded and unswerving—rather than divided and hesitant? What one step can you take today to move in that direction?

Defining Fast and Slow Motion: Necessary and Special Properties
Al-Jurjani defines fast and slow motion relationally and debates the exhaustive nature of their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relational definitions of fast and slow motion and the logical distinction between necessary properties (lāzim musāwī) and special properties (khāṣṣa qāṣira).

Core Idea
Speed is relative; fast and slow are defined by comparison, not in isolation.

In this passage, al-Jurjani unpacks the relational nature of speed. He defines fast and slow motion through their necessary concomitants and then scrutinizes whether certain properties are truly exhaustive. The debate reveals kalam's refined method of analyzing definitions and their logical scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's definitions and the subsequent debate unfold naturally.
Notice the phrase 'إذا قيست' which signals the relational starting point.
2
Identify the two definitions for each motion
Find the two coextensive necessary properties of fast motion and the two properties of slow motion. Note which property of slow motion is called a 'special property' (خاصة قاصرة) and why.
The commentary will help clarify the logical status of each property.
3
Analyze the debate about the special property
The passage presents an objection and answer regarding whether 'cutting less distance in equal time' is a special property of slow motion. Follow the argument to see how the author refines the definition.
This debate touches on divisibility and indivisible parts (atoms).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركة إذا قيست إلى حركة آخر فهي إما سريعة وهي التي تقطع مسافة مساوية لمسافة أخرى في زمان أقل من زمانها ويلزمها أي الحركة السريعة أن تقطع الأكثر أي المسافة التي مقدارها أكثر في الزمان المساوي يعني أنه إذا فرض تساوي الحركتين في المسافة كان زمان السريعة أقل، وإذا فرض تساويهما في الزمان كانت مسافة السريعة أكثر، فهذان الوصفان لازمان مساويان للسريعة ولذلك عرفت بكل واحد منهما، وإما قطعها لمسافة أطول في زمان أقصر فخاصة قاصرة وإما بطيئة وهي التي بالعكس فتقطع المساوي من المسافة في الزمان الأكثر أو تقطع الأقل من المسافة في الزمان المساوي وربما قطعت مسافة أقل في زمان الأكثر لكنه غير شامل لها. قوله: (إذا قيست إلخ) إشارة إلى أن الحركة في نفسها لا تتصف بالسرعة والبطء. قوله: فخاصة قاصرة إلخ) قيل: فيه بحث لأن قطع السريعة في الزمان المساوي مسافة أكثر خاصة شاملة، يلزمها قطعها في زمان أقصر مسافة أكثر، لأن الزيادة على أصل مسافة البطيئة التي قطعها السريعة لفضل سرعتها قابلة للقسمة وإلا لزم الجزء وقطعها في زمان أقصر والجواب: أنه إذا اعتبر بعض الزيادة مع مسافة البطيئة كانت هذه السريعة التي اعتبر في مسافتها كل الزيادة خارجة عن هذه الخاصة بلا شبهة. قوله: (على نهج واحد) لا يختلف بالأخذ والترك والسرعة والبطء بالنظر إلى المبدأ. قوله: (فهي إما سريعة وإما بطيئة) فإن قلت: هاهنا قسم آخر وهي المساوية فلم لم يتعرض له. قلت: لأن هذا تقسيم للحركة باعتبار وصفها الذاتي والمساواة صفة للمقدار أولاً وبالذات. قوله: (فخاصة قاصرة) لأن السريعة التي تقطع المسافة في زمان أقصر لا يصدق عليها هذه الخاصة، وفيه بحث لأن قطع السريعة في الزمان المساوي مسافة أكثر خاصة شاملة ويلزمه قطعها في زمان أقصر مسافة أكثر؛ لأن الزيادة على أصل مسافة البطيء التي قطعها السريعة بفضل سرعتها قابلة للقسمة البتة وإلا لزم الجزء، فحينئذ إذا فرضت تلك الزيادة تمام مسافة السريعة لم تقطع البطيئة، وقطع بعضها في زمان أقصر، فإن قلت: لعل الزيادة لا تتجزئ خارجاً. قلت: بعد تسليم انتفاء لزوم الجزء، فحينئذ إذا فرضت تلك الزيادة تمام مسافة السريعة لم تقطع البطيئة في الزمان المساوي أقل من تلك المسافة، فلم يكن قطع الأقل في الزمان المساوي خاصة شاملة للبطيئة، بل ولا قطع السريعة في الزمان المساوي مسافة أكثر خاصة شاملة للسريعة أيضاً كما لا يخفى اللهم إلا أن يقال تلك المقدار من المسافة لا يقطعها إلا بطيئة لا يتصور أبطأ منها حتى تكون هي أسرع بالنسبة إليه، ففرضها تمام مسافة السريعة فرض لا يمكن مطابقته للواقع وقد يجاب عن البحث بأن سريعاً إذا قطع في جزئين من الزمان غير منقسمين خارجاً مسافة، فالبطيء يقطع في ذلك الزمان مسافة أقصر فليفرض أنها نصف مسافة السريعة، فالزمان القصير هاهنا هو الجزء الواحد فقط، والسريع يقطع فيه مسافة مساوية فقط، وأنت خبير بأن كون الزمان القصير هو الجزء الواحد فقط لا يلائم أصول الفلاسفة كما مرت إليه الإشارة من الشارح في بحث الخلاء.
Motion, when compared to another motion, is either fast—and it is that which traverses a distance equal to another distance in a time less than its time—and it necessarily follows that the fast motion traverses the greater, i.e., the distance whose quantity is greater, in the equal time; meaning that if the two motions are assumed equal in distance, the time of the fast one is less, and if they are assumed equal in time, the distance of the fast one is greater. So these two descriptions are coextensive necessary concomitants of the fast motion, and therefore it is defined by each one of them. And as for its traversing a longer distance in a shorter time, it is a special property. And [motion] is either slow, and it is the opposite: it traverses the equal distance in the greater time, or it traverses the lesser distance in the equal time, and perhaps it traverses a lesser distance in the greater time, but this is not inclusive of it. His saying: 'When it is compared, etc.' indicates that motion in itself is not qualified by fastness or slowness. His saying: 'a special property, etc.' It was said: there is a discussion here because the fact that the fast motion traverses more distance in equal time is a comprehensive property; it necessitates that it traverses more distance in a shorter time, because the increase over the original distance of the slow one which the fast one traversed by virtue of its speed is divisible, otherwise an atom would be entailed, and that it traverses in a shorter time. And the answer: if some of the increase is considered with the distance of the slow one, then this fast motion whose distance includes all the increase is outside this property without doubt. His saying: 'on one course' does not differ by taking and leaving, and speed and slowness with respect to the beginning. His saying: 'so it is either fast or slow' – if you say: there is another division here, namely the equal, so why did he not mention it? I say: because this is a division of motion by virtue of its essential description, and equality is a property of quantity primarily and essentially. His saying: 'a special property' because the fast motion that traverses the distance in a shorter time does not apply to it this property. And there is a discussion here because the fact that the fast motion traverses more distance in equal time is a comprehensive property, and it necessitates that it traverses more distance in a shorter time, because the increase over the original distance of the slow one which the fast one traversed by virtue of its speed is divisible absolutely, otherwise an atom would be entailed. So then if that increase is assumed as the entire distance of the fast one, the slow one would not have traversed in the equal time less than that distance, so 'traversing the lesser in equal time' would not be a comprehensive property for the slow one, nor would 'traversing more in equal time' be a comprehensive property for the fast one, as is not hidden, unless it is said that that amount of distance is not traversed except by a slow one slower than which cannot be conceived, so that it is fast relative to it; therefore, assuming it as the entire distance of the fast one is an assumption that cannot correspond to reality. And it may be answered regarding the discussion that if a fast thing traverses in two indivisible parts of time a distance, then the slow thing traverses in that time a shorter distance, so let it be assumed that it is half the distance of the fast one; then the short time here is only one part, and the fast one traverses in it only an equal distance. And you are aware that the short time being only one part does not conform to the principles of the philosophers, as the commentator has indicated in the discussion of void.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines fast and slow motion relationally and then examines the necessary properties and a debate about the 'special property' (khāṣṣa qāṣira). It begins by stating that when motion is compared to another, it is either fast or slow. Fast motion is defined by two coextensive necessary properties: cutting a distance equal to another in less time, and cutting more distance in equal time. Slow motion is the opposite: cutting equal distance in more time, or cutting less distance in equal time. The commentator then explains that motion in itself is not qualified by speed; speed is relative. A debate arises about the property 'cutting less distance in equal time' being a 'special property' (khāṣṣa qāṣira) of slow motion, meaning it belongs to slow motion exclusively but not to all slow motions (i.e., it is a non-reciprocal property). The objection is that this property might not be truly exclusive if the increase in distance is divisible, leading to atomism. The answer is that if we consider only part of the increase, the property holds for the slow motion. Further discussion involves the assumption of indivisible parts of time and distance, and the final answer invokes the philosophers' principles regarding indivisible parts, noting inconsistency. The passage thus refines the definitional framework for motion and speed.

بطء
خاصة قاصرة
لازم مساو

مسافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'cutting less distance in equal time' is a defining (necessary) property of slow motion, just as the two properties of fast motion are necessary.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this property is a special property (khāṣṣa qāṣira), meaning it is not a necessary concomitant of all slow motions; it is only a non-reciprocal property.

Checkpoint

A) Cutting equal distance in less time and cutting more distance in equal time
B) Cutting more distance in less time and cutting less distance in more time
C) Cutting less distance in equal time and cutting equal distance in more time
D) Cutting equal distance in equal time and cutting more distance in more time

Key Takeaway
Fast and slow motion are defined relationally, and the distinction between necessary coextensive properties and special non-reciprocal properties is central to precise definition in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what areas of your life are you letting comparison define your worth? What would it take for you to stop measuring yourself against others and instead measure yourself against the standard of the Prophet ﷺ? Commit to one specific change today.
?
The passage distinguishes between necessary, coextensive properties and special, non-reciprocal ones. Are there beliefs you hold about your own faith that you assume are universal (like 'a true believer must feel constant joy') that are actually only special circumstances? How can you separate the necessary truths of Islam from your own experiences? Write down one assumption about yourself that you need to test against the Quran and Sunnah.
?
Motion in itself is neither fast nor slow. Your deeds in themselves are not good or bad except by comparison to divine commands. When you excuse a sin as 'small' or a good deed as 'not enough', you are using a relative scale. What would your actions look like if you stopped comparing to others and compared only to what Allah has commanded, no matter how small? What is the smallest step you can take today to align your actions solely to His measure?

Fatherhood as a Relative Concept and the Three Meanings of Idafa
Definition of fatherhood and three meanings of idafa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of fatherhood as a 'repeated relation' and the three distinct meanings of the term idafa in Islamic metaphysics.

Core Idea
Fatherhood is a 'repeated relation' — a relation understood relative to another relation.

This passage from the Fifth Observatory on Relation clarifies what it means for something to be a 'real relative' (mudaf haqiqi). The author explains that fatherhood (al-ubuwwa) has no reality apart from being a relation understood in relation to another relation. He then distinguishes three ways the term idafa is used: for the relation itself, for the thing bearing the relation, and for the composite of both. This careful classification is essential for precise theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Note that the passage includes commentator's remarks.
2
Understand the definition of fatherhood
Focus on the first part: Fatherhood is 'that which is understood in relation to another' and has no reality except that. This is the real relative.
The definition involves a 'repeated relation' – a relation relative to another relation.
3
Identify the three meanings of idafa
The text states that 'idafa' (like 'mudaf') is applied to three meanings: the accident alone, the bearer alone, and the composite of both. List them and note how they relate to the real relative and well-known relative.
This classification resolves potential confusion about the term's usage.
4
Examine the commentator's criticism
Read the commentator's remarks on the author's arrangement. Consider why the separation of relation into a separate observatory is criticized.
The commentator suggests a better arrangement with two chapters in the Fourth Observatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس في الإضافة ] جعل المرصد الرابع في المقولات النسبية واستوفى فيه بيان أحوال الأين على مذهبي المتكلمين ،والحكماء وأفرد منها الإضافة في مرصد على حدة واكتفى في سائر النسب بما مضى في صدر الموقف الثالث، إذ ليس فيها مزيد بحث (وهو مقاصد ) خمسة . المقصد الأول : الأبوة هي المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير ] ( الأبوة هي المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير ولا حقيقة لها إلا ذلك ) أي ليس حقيقتها سوى أنها نسبة معقولة بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى معقولة بالقياس إلى الأولى وحاصلها : النسبة المتكررة كما مر ( وهي الإضافة التي تعد من المقولات وتسمى مضافاً حقيقياً ويقال لذات الأب المعروضة لهذا العارض: إضافة). أيضاً ( وكذا) يقال : الإضافة للمعروض مع العارض وهذان يسميان مضافاً مشهورياً ) فلفظ الإضافة كلفظ المضاف يطلق على ثلاثة معان العارض وحده والمعروض وحده والمجموع المركب منهما قوله : ( المرصد الرابع إلخ) تعريض للمصنف بسوء الترتيب فإن اللائق إدراج ( الإضافة في المرصد الرابع أو جعله منعقدا لمباحث الأين والأمر بين لأنه لا يجوز أن يكون إفرادها لكثرة مباحثها، ولذا لم يتعرض لسوء الترتيب جري بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى والمراد بالغير الغير المخصوص كما مر في تعريفها . قوله : (العارض وحده أي من غير اعتبار المعروض شطراً وكذا الثاني والقرينة مقابلتها للمجموع المركب منهما . قوله : ( جعل المرصد الرابع إلخ فيه إيماء إلى أن ترتيب المصنف ليس بمستحسن فإن الأصوب كما نقل عنه رحمه الله أن يجعل المرصد الرابع فصلين الأول في مباحث الأين لاتفاق الفريقين على تحققه والثاني في الإضافة . قوله : ( ولا حقيقة لها إلخ) أي ليس للأبوة من حيث أنها مضاف حقيقي حقيقة إلا ما ذكر، وإلا فلنفس الأبوة حقيقة مخصوصة غير ما ذكرنا قوله : ( وقد يقال لذات الأب المفروضة إلخ ) قال الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين: الظاهر أن إطلاقه على المعروض من حيث أنه معروض لا من حيث ذاته مع قطع النظر عن المعروض، والفرق بينه وبين المشهوري الآخر أن العارض هاهنا مأخوذ بطريق العروض ولا الجزئية وهناك بالعكس فإن قلت : الأب هو الذات المتصفة بالأبوة لا الذات والأبوة معاً وإلا لم يصدق عليه الحيوان . قلت : المضاف المشهوري هو مفهوم الأب لا ما يصدق عليه وتمام تحقيقه في تلك الحواشي .
[The Fifth Observatory on Relation] He made the Fourth Observatory on relative categories, and fully explained therein the states of where (al-ayn) according to the two schools of the theologians and philosophers, and he singled out relation (idafa) in a separate observatory, and sufficed for the remaining relations with what preceded in the beginning of the Third Stand, because there is no further discussion in them. And it has five objectives. The First Objective: Fatherhood (al-ubuwwa) is that which is understood in relation to another. (Fatherhood is that which is understood in relation to another, and it has no reality except that.) That is, its reality is nothing other than a relation understood in relation to another relation understood in relation to the first. Its sum: the repeated relation, as has passed. And it is the relation which is counted among the categories and is called real relative (mudaf haqiqi). And the essence of the father which bears this accident is also called relation (idafa). Likewise, idafa is said of the bearer together with the accident, and these two are called well-known relative (mudaf mashhuri). So the term idafa, like the term mudaf, is applied to three meanings: the accident alone, the bearer alone, and the composite of both. He (the commentator) said: (The Fourth Observatory, etc.) – this is an implicit criticism of the author for poor arrangement, for it is fitting to include relation in the Fourth Observatory or to devote one part to the investigations of where (al-ayn) and the other to relation. And the matter is unclear, because it is not permissible that its separation be due to the abundance of its investigations. For this reason, he did not criticize the poor arrangement. It proceeds in relation to another relation, and the intended meaning of 'another' is the specific other, as has passed in its definition. He said: (The accident alone) – i.e., without considering the bearer as a part, and likewise the second, and the context contrasts it with the composite of both. He said: (He made the Fourth Observatory, etc.) – in it there is an indication that the arrangement of the author is not commendable, for the more correct, as narrated from him, may Allah have mercy on him, is that the Fourth Observatory should be made into two chapters: the first on the investigations of where, due to the agreement of the two parties on its reality, and the second on relation. He said: (And it has no reality, etc.) – i.e., fatherhood, insofar as it is a real relative, has no reality except what was mentioned; otherwise, fatherhood itself has a specific reality other than what we mentioned. He said: (And it may be said of the essence of the father assumed, etc.) – the commentator said in the glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn: The apparent meaning is that its application to the bearer is by virtue of its being a bearer, not by virtue of its essence without regard to the accident. The difference between it and the other well-known (relative) is that here the accident is taken in the way of inherence, not as part, and there it is the opposite. For if you say: the father is the essence qualified by fatherhood, not the essence and fatherhood together, otherwise it would not be true that it is an animal. I say: The well-known relative is the concept of father, not what it applies to. The full investigation is in those glosses.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification of the concept of 'fatherhood' (al-ubuwwa) as a relative category. It begins by explaining why the author separated the discussion of relation (al-idafa) into a separate observatory: because the Fourth Observatory covered relative categories but focused on 'where' (al-ayn), and relation needed its own treatment. The definition states that fatherhood has no reality beyond being a relation understood in relation to another relation—a 'repeated relation'. This kind of relation is called 'real relative' (mudaf haqiqi). The term 'idafa' (and similarly 'mudaf') is then said to have three distinct meanings: (1) the accident (the relation) alone, (2) the subject (the essence that bears the relation) alone, and (3) the composite of both accident and subject. The first two are also called 'well-known relative' (mudaf mashhuri). The commentator further clarifies with remarks: he criticizes the author's arrangement, noting that it would be better to have two chapters. He also distinguishes between the real relative and the well-known relative: in the real relative, the relation is the accident; in the well-known relative, the term can apply to the bearer or the composite. The passage ends with a dialectical exchange: the objection that 'father' refers only to the essence qualified by fatherhood, not the composite, is answered by stating that the well-known relative is the concept, not the actual referent.

أبوة
إضافة
مضاف حقيقي
مضاف مشهوري
عارض
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'idafa' refers only to the relation itself (the accident).
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'idafa' has three meanings: the accident alone, the bearer alone, and the composite of both.

Checkpoint
How many meanings does the term 'idafa' (relation) have according to this passage?
A) One meaning: the relation itself.
B) Two meanings: the accident and the subject.
C) Three meanings: the accident alone, the subject alone, and the composite of both.
D) Four meanings: including the real relative and the well-known relative.

Key Takeaway
The term idafa has three meanings: the accident (relation) alone, the bearer alone, and the composite of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage defines fatherhood as a 'repeated relation' with no reality beyond that. Does this abstract definition strip fatherhood of its emotional and spiritual meaning, or does it reveal something deeper about how our identities are shaped by relationships? How does this challenge your own understanding of what it means to be a father—or to be someone's child?

The distinction between the 'real relative' (the relation itself) and the 'well-known relative' (the concept of father) invites you to examine how you relate to others. In your most important relationships, do you engage with the person as a 'concept' or as the actual reality bearing that relation? Which leads to more authentic connection?
?
The commentator criticizes the author's arrangement, revealing a concern for precision in categorization. The Definition of al-Mudaf
Defines al-mudaf and clarifies its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam tradition defines a relative entity (al-mudaf) and the crucial distinction between conceiving the other as part of the relative's quiddity versus as a mere concomitant.

Core Idea
A relative is defined by needing another to be conceived, not just by association.

This passage opens the discussion of relation (al-idafah) and the relative (al-mudaf). The author carefully clarifies that the definition does not mean simple entailment — otherwise many non-relative concepts would qualify. Instead, the relative's very quiddity is incomplete without conceiving something external. Get ready to explore this foundational nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Start with the first sentence: 'Relation (al-idafah) is that which is conceived in relation to another.' Then read the note that follows. Let the Arabic sink in before moving to the commentary.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Understand the clarification
The note explains what the definition of al-mudaf does NOT mean. It is not about mere entailment — otherwise evident concomitants (like smoke for fire) would be relatives. The key is that the other is part of the relative's reality.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction matter? The answer: it ensures that only genuinely relational concepts are classified as al-mudaf, preserving the term's precision in logical and metaphysical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : الإضافة هي المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير.
تنبيه : قوله : المضاف ما يعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير لا يراد به أن يلزم من تعقل الغير فإن اللوازم البينة (كذلك) أي هي بحيث يلزم تعقل ملزوماتها فيدخل الماهيات البينة اللوازم في تعريف المضاف (بل) يراد به ( أن يكون من حقيقته تعقل الغير فلا تعقله إلا بتعقل الغير أي هو في حد نفسه بحيث لا يتم تعقل ماهيته إلا بتعقل أمر خارج عنها، وإذا قيد ذلك الغير بكونه نسبة يخرج سائر النسب (و) يبقى (هذا) القول ( يتناول المضاف الحقيقي والقسم الثاني من المشهوري.
يتم قوله : ( أي هي بحيث إلخ) فعبارة المتن إما على حذف المضاف أي ملزوماً للوازم البينة مثل الغير في لزوم تعلقها لتعلق الملزومات . قوله : (من حقيقته تعقل الغير فيه أنه إن أراد أنه بعض حقيقته ففعل الغير على أن من تبعيضه يلزم توقف تعقل كل واحد من المضافين على تعقل الآخر وتقدمه عليه وإن أراد أنه ناشئ من تعقل حقيقة الغير يرد عليه أن لوازم الماهية لذلك وكذا في قوله : لا يتم تعقله إلا بتعقل حمل الباء على السببية يلزم التقدم وإن حمل على الملابسة والملزومات بالنسبة إلى لوازمها البينة فالعبارة غير وافية ببيان المراد والجواب : إن المراد من تتمة تعقل الغير معه لكون ذلك بإزائه، وأن الباقي قوله إلا بتعقل الغير بمعنى مع وتفصيله ما في المباحث المشرقية موافقا للشفاء، إن معنى كون الماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها هو أن يكون الماهية يحوج تعلقها إلى تعقل شيء خارج عنها، وكيف كان فإن الملزومات إذا تصورات تصور معها أن ماهية الملزومات غير مقولة بالقياس إلى ماهيات اللوازم لوجوب كون الماهية التي هي الموضوعات أو الملزومات مستقلة بنفسها، ومتقدمة بذواتها على اللوازم وامتناع كون المضافين كذلك بل أن يكون المعقول المحتاج إلى تعقل غيره لا يتقرر في الذهن ولا في الخارج إلا لأجل وجود ذلك الغير بإزائه . قوله : ( أي هو في حد نفسه إلخ بخلاف القسم الأول من المضاف المشهوري فإنه ليس حد نفسه كذلك بل باعتبار عارضه . قوله : ( وإذا قيد ذلك الغير إلخ وإنما لم يقيد المصنف بذلك لأن مقصوده بيان معنى كونه معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير.
في قوله: (وهذان يسميان مضافاً مشهورياً ) قال في شرح المقاصد : ماوقع في المواقف من أن نفس المعروض أيضاً يسمى مضافاً مشهورياً فخلاف المشهور ، نعم قد يطلق عليه لفظ المضاف بمعنى أنه شيء له الإضافة على ما هو قانون اللغة . قوله : (أي هي بحيث يلزم إلخ) مقتضى السياق أن يقال : فإن الملزومات البينة اللوازم كذلك فأشار الشارح إلى التوجيه بما ذكره الأبهري من أن لفظه ذلك إشارة إلى تعقل الغير لا إلى المجموع ولك أن تحمل عبارة المصنف على حذف المضاف أي ملزومات اللوازم . قوله : ( وإذا قيد ذلك الغير بكونه (نسبة وإنما لم يقيد المصنف هاهنا اعتماداً على ما مر في المرصد الأول من هذا المتن.
The fifth observatory - the first objective: Relation (al-idafah) is that which is conceived in relation to another.
Note: His saying: 'The relative (al-mudaf) is that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another' does not mean that the understanding of the other is entailed, for then the evident concomitants (al-lawazim al-bayyinah) would also be such, i.e., that they are such that the understanding of their correlatives is entailed, thus the quiddities with evident concomitants would enter into the definition of the relative. Rather, it means that it is part of its reality to conceive the other, so you do not conceive it except by conceiving the other, i.e., it is in itself such that the conception of its quiddity is not completed except by conceiving something external to it. And when that other is restricted to being a relation, other relations are excluded. And this statement covers the real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) and the second type of the common relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri).
It completes his saying: (i.e., such that it entails, etc.) So the wording of the text is either ellipsis of the qualified term, i.e., a thing to which the evident concomitants are attached, like the other in the necessity of its involvement because of the involvement of its correlatives. His saying: (from its reality to conceive the other) has a problem: if he means that it is part of its reality, then the other would be a part, and from that partitive sense it would entail the dependence of each of the two relatives on conceiving the other and its priority over it. If he means that it arises from conceiving the reality of the other, then it could be objected that the concomitants of the quiddity are like that. Similarly, in his saying: 'it is not conceived except by conceiving' if the preposition 'bi' is taken as causal it entails priority; if taken as accompaniment (mulabasah), the subject and its evident concomitants are such that the expression does not sufficiently clarify the intended meaning. The answer: What is meant is that the conception of the other completes it because that is parallel to it, and that the remaining part of his saying 'except by conceiving the other' means 'with'. Its detailed explanation is in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyyah in agreement with al-Shifa', that the meaning of a quiddity being predicated in relation to another is that the quiddity requires in its attachment the conception of something external to it. However it may be, when the correlatives are conceived, it is conceived along with them that the quiddity of the correlatives is not predicated in relation to the quiddities of the concomitants, because it is necessary that the quiddity that is the subject or the correlative is independent in itself and prior in its essence to the concomitants, and it is impossible for the two relatives to be like that. Rather, the conceived thing that needs the conception of another is not established in the mind or in the external except because of the existence of that other alongside it. His saying: (i.e., it is in itself, etc.) contrary to the first type of the common relative, for it is not like that in itself, but rather by virtue of an accident. His saying: (And when that other is restricted, etc.) And the author did not restrict it here because his purpose is to explain the meaning of being conceived in relation to another.
Regarding his saying: (And these two are called the common relative). It was said in Sharh al-Maqasid: What occurred in al-Mawaqif that the very substrate is also called the common relative contradicts the well-known. Indeed, the term 'relative' may be applied to it in the sense that it is something that has relation according to the rule of language. His saying: (i.e., such that it entails, etc.) The context requires that it be said: 'For the correlatives with evident concomitants are also like that.' So the commentator pointed to the justification by what al-Abhari mentioned: that his word 'that' refers to the conception of the other, not to the whole. You may also interpret the author's expression as ellipsis of the qualified term, i.e., the things to which the concomitants are attached. His saying: (And when that other is restricted to being a relation) And the author did not restrict it here, relying on what has passed in the first observatory of this text.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification of the concept of 'al-mudaf' (the relative) and its two types: the real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) and the common relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri). The passage begins by stating the first objective: 'Relation (al-idafah) is that which is conceived in relation to another.' Then a note (tanbih) explains the author's definition of 'al-mudaf' as 'that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another.' It clarifies that this does not mean that conceiving the other is a necessary concomitant, because then evident concomitants would also be considered relatives. Rather, it means that the relative's very reality includes the conception of the other, so that its quiddity cannot be fully conceived without conceiving something external to it. This definition includes both the real relative and the second type of the common relative. The passage then addresses a potential problem: whether the phrase 'from its reality to conceive the other' means that the other is part of the relative's reality or that it arises from conceiving the other. The answer is that the other is conceived along with it, as a parallel. The passage also discusses why the author did not restrict 'that other' to being a relation, and includes a remark from Sharh al-Maqasid about the common relative, noting that calling the substrate itself a common relative is contrary to the well-known view.

Key Terms
المضاف (al-mudaf)
الإضافة (al-idafah)
اللوازم البينة (al-lawazim al-bayyinah)
الماهية (al-mahiyyah)
المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير (al-ma'qulah bi al-qiyas ila al-ghayr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any concept that entails another (e.g., fire entails smoke) counts as a relative.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only those whose quiddity itself requires conceiving the other are relatives; mere entailment is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does the definition of al-mudaf mean?
A) The mudaf is any concept that necessarily brings another concept to mind.
B) The mudaf's quiddity cannot be understood without understanding something external to it.
C) The mudaf is a concept that is caused by another.
D) The mudaf is a concept that is similar to another.

Key Takeaway
A relative (mudaf) is defined by its quiddity requiring the conception of another, not by merely implying another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever define yourself or your worth by your relationships to others (e.g., parent, spouse, career)? How might that be like the 'quiddity' of a relative—needing another to be conceived? What would it mean for your identity to be grounded in something that does not require another?

Think of a conviction about your faith that you hold mainly because it is 'related' to something else (e.g., you believe in God because of a logical proof). If that relation were removed, would the conviction still stand? How does this passage challenge you to examine whether your beliefs are part of your quiddity or merely evident concomitants?
?
The passage distinguishes between something that internally requires another to be conceived and something that merely implies another. In your spiritual life, are there actions or states (like prayer or gratitude) that you do only because they imply something else (e.g., showing piety), or are they intrinsically part of your relationship with God? Commit to one area where you will make an external relation internal.

What Is Kalam?

Real and Common Relative: The Two Types
Classifies al-mudaf into two types and clarifies the second type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of al-mudaf into two types: the real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) and the common relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri), and the specific characteristics of each.

Core Idea
Relatives are either essentially so or accidentally so.

Having established what a relative is, the author now introduces its two types. The real relative is one whose quiddity is inherently relational, while the common relative is relational due to a secondary attribute. A special note also addresses which type fits the definition given earlier. This classification is vital for applying the concept correctly in logical and metaphysical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the division statement
Read the sentence: 'وهذا القول يتناول المضاف الحقيقي والقسم الثاني من المشهوري.' Understand that the earlier definition covers both the real relative and the second kind of common relative.
The 'first kind' of common relative is excluded from this definition.
2
Distinguish the two types
The real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) is inherently relational. The common relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri) is relational by accident, such as 'father' when considered with a relation. Only the second kind of common relative fits the strict definition.
A 'common relative' is something that can be considered both in itself and relationally.
3
Note the Sharh al-Maqasid remark
The passage includes a note from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifying a point about the common relative: calling the substrate itself a common relative is contrary to common scholarly convention, though allowed linguistically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : الإضافة هي المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير.
تنبيه : قوله : المضاف ما يعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير لا يراد به أن يلزم من تعقل الغير فإن اللوازم البينة (كذلك) أي هي بحيث يلزم تعقل ملزوماتها فيدخل الماهيات البينة اللوازم في تعريف المضاف (بل) يراد به ( أن يكون من حقيقته تعقل الغير فلا تعقله إلا بتعقل الغير أي هو في حد نفسه بحيث لا يتم تعقل ماهيته إلا بتعقل أمر خارج عنها، وإذا قيد ذلك الغير بكونه نسبة يخرج سائر النسب (و) يبقى (هذا) القول ( يتناول المضاف الحقيقي والقسم الثاني من المشهوري.
يتم قوله : ( أي هي بحيث إلخ) فعبارة المتن إما على حذف المضاف أي ملزوماً للوازم البينة مثل الغير في لزوم تعلقها لتعلق الملزومات . قوله : (من حقيقته تعقل الغير فيه أنه إن أراد أنه بعض حقيقته ففعل الغير على أن من تبعيضه يلزم توقف تعقل كل واحد من المضافين على تعقل الآخر وتقدمه عليه وإن أراد أنه ناشئ من تعقل حقيقة الغير يرد عليه أن لوازم الماهية لذلك وكذا في قوله : لا يتم تعقله إلا بتعقل حمل الباء على السببية يلزم التقدم وإن حمل على الملابسة والملزومات بالنسبة إلى لوازمها البينة فالعبارة غير وافية ببيان المراد والجواب : إن المراد من تتمة تعقل الغير معه لكون ذلك بإزائه، وأن الباقي قوله إلا بتعقل الغير بمعنى مع وتفصيله ما في المباحث المشرقية موافقا للشفاء، إن معنى كون الماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها هو أن يكون الماهية يحوج تعلقها إلى تعقل شيء خارج عنها، وكيف كان فإن الملزومات إذا تصورات تصور معها أن ماهية الملزومات غير مقولة بالقياس إلى ماهيات اللوازم لوجوب كون الماهية التي هي الموضوعات أو الملزومات مستقلة بنفسها، ومتقدمة بذواتها على اللوازم وامتناع كون المضافين كذلك بل أن يكون المعقول المحتاج إلى تعقل غيره لا يتقرر في الذهن ولا في الخارج إلا لأجل وجود ذلك الغير بإزائه . قوله : ( أي هو في حد نفسه إلخ بخلاف القسم الأول من المضاف المشهوري فإنه ليس حد نفسه كذلك بل باعتبار عارضه . قوله : ( وإذا قيد ذلك الغير إلخ وإنما لم يقيد المصنف بذلك لأن مقصوده بيان معنى كونه معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير.
في قوله: (وهذان يسميان مضافاً مشهورياً ) قال في شرح المقاصد : ماوقع في المواقف من أن نفس المعروض أيضاً يسمى مضافاً مشهورياً فخلاف المشهور ، نعم قد يطلق عليه لفظ المضاف بمعنى أنه شيء له الإضافة على ما هو قانون اللغة . قوله : (أي هي بحيث يلزم إلخ) مقتضى السياق أن يقال : فإن الملزومات البينة اللوازم كذلك فأشار الشارح إلى التوجيه بما ذكره الأبهري من أن لفظه ذلك إشارة إلى تعقل الغير لا إلى المجموع ولك أن تحمل عبارة المصنف على حذف المضاف أي ملزومات اللوازم . قوله : ( وإذا قيد ذلك الغير بكونه (نسبة وإنما لم يقيد المصنف هاهنا اعتماداً على ما مر في المرصد الأول من هذا المتن.
The fifth observatory - the first objective: Relation (al-idafah) is that which is conceived in relation to another.
Note: His saying: 'The relative (al-mudaf) is that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another' does not mean that the understanding of the other is entailed, for then the evident concomitants (al-lawazim al-bayyinah) would also be such, i.e., that they are such that the understanding of their correlatives is entailed, thus the quiddities with evident concomitants would enter into the definition of the relative. Rather, it means that it is part of its reality to conceive the other, so you do not conceive it except by conceiving the other, i.e., it is in itself such that the conception of its quiddity is not completed except by conceiving something external to it. And when that other is restricted to being a relation, other relations are excluded. And this statement covers the real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) and the second type of the common relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri).
It completes his saying: (i.e., such that it entails, etc.) So the wording of the text is either ellipsis of the qualified term, i.e., a thing to which the evident concomitants are attached, like the other in the necessity of its involvement because of the involvement of its correlatives. His saying: (from its reality to conceive the other) has a problem: if he means that it is part of its reality, then the other would be a part, and from that partitive sense it would entail the dependence of each of the two relatives on conceiving the other and its priority over it. If he means that it arises from conceiving the reality of the other, then it could be objected that the concomitants of the quiddity are like that. Similarly, in his saying: 'it is not conceived except by conceiving' if the preposition 'bi' is taken as causal it entails priority; if taken as accompaniment (mulabasah), the subject and its evident concomitants are such that the expression does not sufficiently clarify the intended meaning. The answer: What is meant is that the conception of the other completes it because that is parallel to it, and that the remaining part of his saying 'except by conceiving the other' means 'with'. Its detailed explanation is in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyyah in agreement with al-Shifa', that the meaning of a quiddity being predicated in relation to another is that the quiddity requires in its attachment the conception of something external to it. However it may be, when the correlatives are conceived, it is conceived along with them that the quiddity of the correlatives is not predicated in relation to the quiddities of the concomitants, because it is necessary that the quiddity that is the subject or the correlative is independent in itself and prior in its essence to the concomitants, and it is impossible for the two relatives to be like that. Rather, the conceived thing that needs the conception of another is not established in the mind or in the external except because of the existence of that other alongside it. His saying: (i.e., it is in itself, etc.) contrary to the first type of the common relative, for it is not like that in itself, but rather by virtue of an accident. His saying: (And when that other is restricted, etc.) And the author did not restrict it here because his purpose is to explain the meaning of being conceived in relation to another.
Regarding his saying: (And these two are called the common relative). It was said in Sharh al-Maqasid: What occurred in al-Mawaqif that the very substrate is also called the common relative contradicts the well-known. Indeed, the term 'relative' may be applied to it in the sense that it is something that has relation according to the rule of language. His saying: (i.e., such that it entails, etc.) The context requires that it be said: 'For the correlatives with evident concomitants are also like that.' So the commentator pointed to the justification by what al-Abhari mentioned: that his word 'that' refers to the conception of the other, not to the whole. You may also interpret the author's expression as ellipsis of the qualified term, i.e., the things to which the concomitants are attached. His saying: (And when that other is restricted to being a relation) And the author did not restrict it here, relying on what has passed in the first observatory of this text.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification of the concept of 'al-mudaf' (the relative) and its two types: the real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) and the common relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri). The passage begins by stating the first objective: 'Relation (al-idafah) is that which is conceived in relation to another.' Then a note (tanbih) explains the author's definition of 'al-mudaf' as 'that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another.' It clarifies that this does not mean that conceiving the other is a necessary concomitant, because then evident concomitants would also be considered relatives. Rather, it means that the relative's very reality includes the conception of the other, so that its quiddity cannot be fully conceived without conceiving something external to it. This definition includes both the real relative and the second type of the common relative. The passage then addresses a potential problem: whether the phrase 'from its reality to conceive the other' means that the other is part of the relative's reality or that it arises from conceiving the other. The answer is that the other is conceived along with it, as a parallel. The passage also discusses why the author did not restrict 'that other' to being a relation, and includes a remark from Sharh al-Maqasid about the common relative, noting that calling the substrate itself a common relative is contrary to the well-known view.

Key Terms
المضاف الحقيقي (al-mudaf al-haqiqi)
المضاف المشهوري (al-mudaf al-mashhuri)
الإضافة (al-idafah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type(s) of al-mudaf are covered by the definition 'whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another'?
A) Only the real relative.
B) The real relative and the first type of common relative.
C) The real relative and the second type of common relative.
D) All types of relatives.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-mudaf applies only to the real relative and the second type of common relative, not to things that are merely spoken of relationally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe your relationship with Allah as 'servant' or 'worshipper', do you treat those terms as real relatives (requiring Him) or as common relatives (just labels)? How does this lesson challenge you to be more precise in your understanding?
?
Think of a time you used a term about your faith loosely, without considering its technical meaning. How can you commit to studying the precise definitions of key Islamic terms, starting with this lesson?
?

What Is Kalam?

Definition of Shape and Proof that the Atom Has No Shape
Defines shape and proves that the indivisible atom has no shape because shape requires parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of shape in kalam and why the indivisible atom cannot have a shape.

Core Idea
Shape requires parts; the atom has no parts — therefore, no shape.

The mutakallimun agreed that the indivisible atom has no shape. But why? Their reasoning is based on a precise definition of shape. In this lesson, we will explore that definition and follow the logical proof that leads to the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with a consensus statement and then gives a definition.
2
Understand the definition of shape
Shape is defined as a configuration of boundaries. There are two types: a single boundary (sphere) or multiple boundaries (polygon).
This definition is key to the proof.
3
Follow the proof
Shape only applies to something that has a part, because a boundary is an extremity, and an extremity is only conceivable in relation to something having an extremity. Thus, shape implies at least two parts. Since the atom is indivisible and has no parts, it cannot have shape.
Consider why an extremity necessarily implies two parts.
4
Reflect and verify
Ask yourself: Why did the mutakallimun agree on this? Can you think of any counterexample? The proof is purely logical and depends on the definition.
This reinforces the connection between definition and proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوهر الفرد لا شكل له باتفاق المتكلمين لأنه الشكل هيئة إحاطة حد واحد وهو الكرة أو حدود وهو المضلع، ولا ذلك إلا فيما له جزء فإن الحد هو النهاية ولا تعقل النهاية إلا بالنسبة إلى ذي نهاية فيكون هناك لا محالة جزآن
The indivisible atom has no shape by agreement of the mutakallimun, because shape is a configuration of a single boundary—which is the sphere—or boundaries—which is the polygon—and that only applies to something that has a part, for the boundary is the extremity, and the extremity is only conceivable in relation to something having an extremity, so there are inevitably two parts.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by stating the agreed-upon position: the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) has no shape. The proof is based on the definition of shape (al-shakl): it is the configuration (hay'a) of a single boundary (hadd wāḥid), namely the sphere (kura), or multiple boundaries (ḥudūd), namely a polygon (muḍalla'). Shape only applies to something with parts (juz'), because a boundary is an extremity (nihāya), and an extremity is only conceivable in relation to something having an extremity. Therefore, if the atom had a shape, it would necessarily have two parts (juz'ān), each being an extremity of the whole. But the atom is indivisible and has no parts, so it can have no shape.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الشكل
الهيئة
الحد
النهاية
الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'shape' simply means having an external form, regardless of parts.
Correction: In kalam, shape is defined strictly as requiring boundaries and parts. The atom lacks parts, so it cannot have shape even if it appears to occupy space.

Checkpoint
Why does the indivisible atom have no shape according to the mutakallimun?
A) Because it is too small to have a shape.
B) Because shape requires boundaries and parts, but the atom has no parts.
C) Because shape only applies to compound bodies, not simple substances.
D) Because the atom is not a physical entity.

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom has no shape because shape requires boundaries and at least two parts, which the atom lacks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of an atom or a basic particle, do you instinctively imagine it as having a shape? How does this lesson's logical definition challenge your mental picture?
?
This proof relies on precise definitions. In your own life, where do you make assumptions about things without carefully defining them? What harm could that cause?
?
The mutakallimun agreed on this because of logical necessity. Is there an area of your belief or knowledge where you accept something based on consensus without understanding the proof? How can you begin to seek that understanding?

Defining Natural Body: The Role of Possible Dimensions
Al-Sanusi defines natural body and justifies the condition of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of natural body in Islamic philosophy and why the possibility of dimensions is essential to that definition.

Core Idea
A natural body is not defined by actual dimensions, but by the possibility of supposing them.

Before we can discuss the properties of bodies, we need a clear definition of what a natural body is. The philosophers define it as a substance in which three dimensions can be supposed to exist. In this passage, al-Sanusi explains why they condition definition with possibility, not actuality, and what this distinction means for our understanding of corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition and its translation. Notice the key phrase 'يمكن أن يفرض' (can be supposed).
The definition is: جوهر يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة متقاطعة على زوايا قائمة.
2
Understand why actual dimensions are not required
The author explains that actual dimensions are not necessary for the essence of body. You can conceive of a body without actual length, width, and depth.
Only the possibility of supposing them is necessary.
3
Compare with line and surface
Note why line and surface are rejected as defining features: line does not exist in many bodies, and surface is necessary for existence but not for essence.
A body can be infinite, thus having no surface.
4
Grasp the summary
The author summarizes that the definition considers only the supposition (farḍ) of dimensions, not their actual existence.
This avoids problems with infinite bodies or bodies without lines.

Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of natural body (al-jism al-ṭabīʿī) from the First Observatory on Body in Sharḥ al-Mawāqif. The author begins by stating the chapter and its aims. The first aim is the definition of body and its parts. He explains that the term 'body' is used by philosophers with lexical polysemy (al-ishtirāk al-lafẓī) for two meanings: natural body and mathematical body. The natural body is defined as 'a substance in which three dimensions intersecting at right angles can be supposed.' The key phrase is 'can be supposed' (yumkin an yufraḍ), not actual existence. The author emphasizes that actual dimensions are not necessary for the essence of natural body; only the possibility (imkān) of supposing them is required. He then addresses the question: why not define body by line or surface? Line is not necessary for body because it does not exist in many bodies, especially spheres. Surface, though necessary for the existence of body (since every finite body must be bounded by a surface), is not necessary for the essence (māhiyya) of body, because one can conceive of an infinite body without a surface. Therefore, defining body by surface would not capture its essence. The author summarizes that only the supposition (al-farḍ) of dimensions is considered in the definition, not their actual existence. He then provides a detailed analysis of the terms farḍ (supposition) and imkān (possibility), distinguishing between the possibility of the act of supposing and the possibility of the supposed dimensions. He critiques alternative interpretations and concludes that the correct approach is to consider the possibility of supposing the three dimensions, as this does not entail any specific shape (shakl) and avoids the problems of requiring actual dimensions or arbitrary suppositions. The passage ends with a discussion on finitude (tanāhī) in position and magnitude, clarifying that body necessarily ends with an actual surface due to its finitude in magnitude, but line and surface may lack an actual end if they are infinite in position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a natural body must actually have three dimensions (length, width, depth) to be a body.
Correction: The definition requires only the possibility of supposing those dimensions, not their actual existence. A body could be a point-like substance (in kalam) or infinite; it remains a body.

Checkpoint

A) Because actual dimensions are impossible in a natural body
B) Because actual dimensions are not necessary for the essence of body
C) Because only the surface is actual
D) Because dimensions are not real

Key Takeaway
A natural body is defined by the possibility of supposing three perpendicular dimensions, not by their actual existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
We often define ourselves by our 'surfaces' – our roles, achievements, and limits. But this lesson shows that the essence of a body is not removed even if it is infinite and lacks a surface. What are the 'surfaces' in your life that you have mistaken for your essence? How would your relationship with Allah transform if you saw yourself as defined only by your potential (your 'imkan') rather than by what you have actually attained?
?
The definition of natural body is built on what can be supposed, not on what is already actual. In your spiritual life, do you focus more on your actual shortcomings or on the infinite possibility of Allah's mercy and your own capacity for change? Which perspective brings you closer to sincere repentance and hope?
?
If a body can be conceived as infinite and still be a body, then your current state – no matter how limited or broken – does not negate your fundamental identity as a servant of Allah. What specific belief about yourself have you been clinging to that actually contradicts this truth? What step can you take today to act on the possibility of becoming better, rather than being paralyzed by your actual flaws?

What Is Kalam?

10 min
Defining the Natural Body: Potential Dimensions
Sanusi defends the definition of body via potential dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classical definition of a natural body in kalām relies on the possibility of supposing dimensions rather than on actual dimensions.

Core Idea
A body is defined by what it can have, not what it actually has.

The definition of a body might seem straightforward—something with length, width, and depth. However, the kalām tradition recognized that actual dimensions are not always present, as seen in a sphere where three perpendicular lines are not actually found. In this passage, Sanusi explains why the definition must be based on the possibility of supposing dimensions, not their actual existence, and clarifies related misconceptions about finitude and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument around the necessity of potential dimensions.
Pay attention to the terms 'imkān al-farḍ' (possibility of supposition) and 'al-buʿd' (dimension).
2
Identify why actual lines and surfaces are not essential
The passage explains that a line may not exist in a sphere, and a surface is not necessary for the essence of a body. Find these reasons and see how they support the definition using potentiality.
The sphere example is key—it shows a body without actual perpendicular lines.
3
Understand the distinction between supposition as permission and estimation
Sanusi clarifies that 'supposition' (farḍ) here means permission (tajwīz), not estimation (taqdīr). This affects how we understand the possibility of dimensions. Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the definition?
If supposition were estimation, the intellect could impose dimensions on abstract entities, which is not intended.

Commentary
This passage defines the natural body (al-jism al-ṭabīʿī) and justifies why its definition relies on the possibility of supposing dimensions (imkān al-farḍ) rather than actual dimensions. It begins by stating the topic: the first observatory, first chapter, first objective – definition of body and its parts. The author discusses the phrase 'they are three intersecting at right angles' from the matn. The commentary then addresses why the definition does not require actual lines or surfaces. The line (al-khaṭṭ) is not necessary for the existence of the body, especially in a sphere where no line exists. The surface (al-saṭḥ) might be necessary for the body's existence due to finitude, but it is not necessary for its essence, since a body could be infinite in all directions. Therefore, defining the body by line or surface is invalid. The summary is that the definition considers only the possibility of supposing dimensions, not actuality. The commentator clarifies that 'supposition' here means permission (al-tajwīz) not estimation (al-taqdīr), and it does not apply to abstract entities. The passage then discusses finitude of dimensions and explains that the body's essence does not depend on actual dimensions, as they may change while the body remains. The conclusion is that the definition is based on the potentiality of dimensions, not their actual presence.

Objection: Does Potential Dimensions Include Abstract Substances?
Objection and answer regarding the definition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of body requires both possibility and the condition of supposition, and how the objection regarding abstract substances is refuted.

Core Idea
Possibility alone would admit abstract substances as bodies; supposition excludes them.

This passage addresses a key objection to the definition of body. An objector argues that adding the condition of 'supposition' is problematic because it might include abstract substances. The response clarifies that supposition of dimensions in abstract substances is impossible, and further that some bodies (like celestial spheres) cannot have the supposed dimensions actually, so both conditions are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's claim
Read the objection carefully: the condition of supposition plus possibility is harmful because it would include abstract substances, which are not bodies.
The objector thinks that because we can suppose dimensions in abstract substances, they would be considered bodies.
2
Examine the first part of the answer
The author states that abstract substances cannot have dimensions even as a supposition. So the objection is unfounded.
This is based on Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid.
3
Examine the second part of the answer
Even if possibility alone were enough, celestial spheres cannot have the supposed dimensions actually, so the condition of supposition is necessary to exclude them.
This refutes the idea that possibility alone suffices.
4
Synthesize why both conditions are needed
Pairing possibility with supposition ensures that abstract substances (impossible even as supposition) and celestial spheres (impossible actually) are excluded from the definition of body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واكتفى بإمكان الفرض قيل عليه قيد الفرض مع الإمكان غير مفيد بل مخل لأنه يدخل حينئذ ما قصد إخراجه . أعني الجواهر المجردة، لأنَّ فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيها ممكن غاية الأمر أن يكون المفروض محالاً، وأقول : إما حديث الأخلال فيه فعدما في شرح المقاصد من أنه المجردات يستحيل فرض الأبعاد بمعنى أن اتصافها بها من المحالات التي لا يمكن فرضها، وأما ما أشار إليه من كفاية قيد الإمكان فهو مع أنه مأخوذ من شرح المقاصد حيث قال: والظاهر أنه يكفي الإمكان أو القابلية ولا حاجة إلى اعتبار الفرض مدفوع بأن بعض الأجسام لا يمكن فيه الأبعاد المفروضة المذكورة بالفعل كالأفلاك التي تستلزم أشكالها استلزاماً ذاتياً .
His saying: 'And he sufficed with the possibility of supposition.' It was said to him that the condition of supposition along with possibility is not beneficial but rather harmful, because then it includes what he intended to exclude, namely the abstract substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada), because the supposition of the three dimensions in them is possible, the only issue being that the supposed is impossible. And I say: As for the talk about harm in it, it is absent in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, that abstract substances cannot have supposition of dimensions in the sense that their being described by them is among the impossibilities that cannot be supposed. As for what he indicated about the sufficiency of the condition of possibility, it is, despite being taken from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid where he said: 'And it appears that possibility or potentiality suffices, and there is no need to consider supposition,' it is refuted because some bodies cannot have the mentioned supposed dimensions actually, such as the celestial spheres, whose shapes entail them essentially.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of body. The objector argues that including the condition of supposition (qayd al-farḍ) along with possibility (al-imkān) is not only useless but harmful, because it would include abstract substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada) within the definition of body. Since one can suppose dimensions in abstract substances (even if such dimensions are impossible in reality), they would be considered bodies. The answer, drawn from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, is twofold. First, the notion of harm is unfounded because abstract substances cannot have dimensions even as a supposition; it is impossible for them to be described by dimensions. Second, the sufficiency of possibility alone is refuted by the case of celestial spheres (al-aflāk), which, due to their essential shapes, cannot actually have the supposed perpendicular dimensions, but they are still bodies. Therefore, the condition of supposition is necessary to exclude such cases where possibility alone would admit bodies that do not meet the definition.

Key Terms
جواهر مجردة
أفلاك
فرض
إمكان
قابلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility alone is sufficient to define body; the condition of supposition is unnecessary.
Correction: Possibility alone would include celestial spheres which cannot have the supposed dimensions actually, so supposition is needed.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector claim that the condition of supposition is harmful?
A) Because it excludes celestial spheres from being bodies.
B) Because it would include abstract substances, which are not bodies.
C) Because it makes the definition too narrow.
D) Because supposition is impossible for any entity.

Key Takeaway
The condition of supposition (farḍ) is necessary in the definition of body to exclude both abstract substances (which cannot have dimensions even in supposition) and celestial spheres (which cannot have the supposed dimensions actually).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define concepts in your faith—like the attributes of Allah—do you rush to include what seems possible without checking the necessary conditions that protect the truth? How might this passage warn you against laziness in theological precision?
?
What are the 'celestial spheres' in your own understanding of Islam—ideas that seem to fit a definition but, upon closer inspection, require extra conditions to be correctly placed? Will you now commit to examining your beliefs with the same rigor as the author?
?
This passage shows that even a mental supposition (farḍ) can be impossible for some realities. Are there aspects of Allah's essence or attributes that you have tried to imagine or compare to creation, even in your mind? How will you guard against such suppositions from now on?
